The thirty-second chapter.
On the extraction of teeth erupted on others.
The teeth, if they erupt on other than their natural course, the form becomes ugly, especially if this happens in women and the delicate, so you should look, if the tooth has erupted from behind another tooth.
And it is not possible to spread it or return it, extract it, and if it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this tool, which is this image:
And it resembles the small beak, and let it be of Indian steel, very sharp-tipped, and your cutting of it should be on many days due to the hardness of the tooth and so as not to shake the others of the teeth, and as for if it is prominent and firmly established, then file it with a file from India, which is this image.
It is all of Indian [steel] and its hilt of it is very finely engraved, like the rasp with which the needles are made, file the tooth slowly, slowly, on many days gently, lest the tooth shake and fall, then smooth it finally and strip it with some scrapers.
And if a tooth has broken off some of it, and it hurts the tongue when speaking, it is necessary to file it as well, until the roughness of that break disappears and it becomes even and smooth, and does not hurt the tongue nor spoil the speech.
Chapter Thirty-Third.
On ligating the loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the anterior teeth become loose and mobile due to a blow or a fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything to eat, so that they do not fall out, and the astringent medicines did not benefit them in the treatment.
The trick in this is to tie them with a gold or silver thread, and gold is better, because silver tarnishes and corrupts after some days, while gold remains in its state forever and does not suffer that, and the thread should be of medium fineness and thickness according to the space between the teeth.
And the form of the ligature is that you take the thread and enter its bend between the sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, one or more, until you reach the sound tooth on the other side.
Then you return the weaving to the side from which you started, and tighten your hand gently and wisely so that it does not move at all, and your tightening of the thread should be at the roots of the teeth so that it does not slip out, then you cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors and gather them and twist them with lint and hide them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
So that the tongue is not hurt, then leave it tied like this for as long as it remains, and if it loosens or breaks, tie it with another thread, and he will benefit from it forever like this, and this is the form of the teeth and the manner of the ligature in two sound teeth and two loose teeth as you see:.
And you may return the one or two teeth after they have fallen in their place and ligate them as we described, and they will remain, and only a skilled craftsman does that, and he may carve a bone from the bones of the ox and make it in the form of a tooth and place it in the place from which the tooth has gone.
And he ties it as we said, and it remains and he benefits from that,. 
Chapter Thirty-Fourth.
On cutting the ligament that appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament that appears under the tongue may be either natural, which the person is born with, or it may be accidental from a wound that has healed.
The work in it is that you open the patient's mouth and his head is in your lap, and you raise his tongue, then you cut that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its restraint, and if there is some hardness and complexity in it and that is from the healing of a wound, then you split it with a probe and cut it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is loosened.
And beware that the incision is not too deep into the flesh, for you may cut an artery there and bleeding will occur, then he rinses with rose water or vinegar and cold water, then place a cotton wick under the tongue which the patient holds every night.
So that it does not heal again, and if bleeding occurs, place powdered vitriol on the place, and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery that is suitable for that.
Then treat it with the rest of the appropriate treatment until it heals,. 
Chapter Thirty-Fifth.
On removing the frog that forms under the tongue.
There may occur under the tongue a swelling resembling a small frog that prevents the tongue from performing its natural function, and it may grow until it fills the mouth, and the work in it is that you open the patient's mouth towards the sun and look at the swelling.
If you see it dark in color or black and hard, the patient does not feel it, so do not touch it, for it is a cancer, and if it is inclined to whiteness and has moisture in it, split it with a probe and cut it with a fine scalpel and remove it from all sides.
If the blood overwhelms you during your work, place powdered vitriol on it until the bleeding stops, then return to your work until you extract it completely, then he rinses with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with the rest of the appropriate treatment until it heals,.
The thirty-sixth chapter.
On the treatment of tonsil swelling.
And what grows in the throat of other swellings may occur inside the throat, glands resembling the glands that occur from the outside, called tonsils. If you treat them with what I mentioned in the division, and they do not heal.
So look, if the swelling is hard, of a pale color, and has little sensation, do not expose it to the iron. And if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not expose it to the iron either, for fear of bleeding blood, but leave it until it ripens, either you incise it or it bursts of itself.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut, and the work in it is that you look before the operation if its hot swelling has settled completely or decreased somewhat.
Then sit the patient next to the sun, with his head in your lap, and open his mouth, and take a servant in front of you who presses his tongue down with this tool:.
Made of silver or copper, it is thin like a knife. If you press the tongue with it and the swelling becomes visible to your eye, take a hook and insert it into the tonsil and pull it out as much as possible without pulling anything of the membranes with it.
Then cut it with a tool that looks like scissors, except that its two ends are bent so that the mouth of each one is opposite the other, very sharp, made of Indian iron or Damascus steel:.
If this tool is not available, then cut it with a lancet that looks like this:.
Sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same way, then after cutting, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If he has a hemorrhage, gargle with water that has boiled pomegranate peels or myrtle leaves or the like of astringents until the bleeding is stopped, then treat him until he is cured. And other swellings may grow in the throat other than the tonsils, you cut them as I mentioned in cutting the tonsils, alike.
And I treated a woman from a swelling that had grown inside her throat, tending towards paleness, with little sensation, almost blocking the throat, and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and it had prevented her from eating and drinking water.
And she was on the verge of death if she had remained for a day or two, and the swelling had two branches protruding from it until they came out of her nostrils. I quickly inserted a hook into one of them and pulled it, and a good piece was pulled out.
Then I cut it where I reached it from the nostril opening, then I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril opening, then I opened her mouth and pressed her tongue, then I inserted the hook into the swelling itself and cut part of it, and only a small amount of blood flowed.
The woman's throat was opened, and she hurried at that moment to drink water, then she took food. We continued to cut from that swelling repeatedly for a long time, and the swelling would recur in place of what was cut, until that prolonged for me and for her.
So I resorted to cauterizing the swelling inside the throat, so it stopped increasing, then I traveled from that place and did not know what God did with her after me.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On the excision of the uvular tumor.
If a descent of the fluxion occurs to the uvula and it becomes swollen and is white and elongated, it is called a "column"; and if it is thick at the bottom and rounded, it is called a "grape". If it is treated with what we mentioned in the division, and the treatment does not benefit,
and I see that the hot tumor has subsided from it and it has become thin, it is necessary to cut it. And what of it is collected, rounded, and does not have a length, and is bloody or of a dark color or has no sensation, it is necessary to avoid cutting it.
For in it there is a risk to the patient, so if you see it in the manner mentioned, of its whiteness and length, it is necessary for the patient to sit facing the sun, and you press his tongue with the instrument described earlier, then you insert the hook into the grape.
And you pull it down and cut it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the section on tonsillectomy. And it is necessary not to cut from it except what has exceeded the natural order without excess, for if you cut more from it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the cutting, use what we described in the tonsillectomy section to treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of cutting it, it is necessary to use the method of cauterizing it without fear or caution, and the direction of the cauterization in it is by the sharp medicine, which is that you place the patient's head in your lap.
Then you press his tongue with the instrument mentioned, then you take from the sharp water mentioned in the cauterization chapter and knead with it unslaked lime, and you make it neither thick nor thin, and you fill the recess of this instrument with it, and this is its shape:
Its end in which you place the medicine has a recess like the recess of a spatula. And you place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient is lying on his side so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat, lest something descends from it to his throat and harms him.
Then you hold your hand with the medicine and press it on the uvula for about half an hour until you see it has turned black and the burning of the medicine has subsided. And if you wish, you can take a cotton swab and wrap it around the end of a spatula, and wet the cotton in the medicine.
And you insert the spatula with the cotton into a tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. You do this repeatedly until you reach what you want from the cauterization of the grape, then you leave it, for it will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, you reapply it.
After the cauterization, wipe around the grape with a cotton swab soaked in ghee and dry with it what is around it from the medicine, then he rinses with cold water and is treated externally with fomentations and internally with gargles until he heals. This type of treatment is safer than excision and further from fear.
And the uvula can also be treated with what is gentler than cauterization and excision, treated by fumigation in this manner: Take fenugreek, hyssop, thyme, rue, wormwood, chamomile, and the like of the herbs, gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
And boil them, and the pot is sealed with wax, and there is a hole in the middle of the lid to which the hollow instrument is attached in this way: It is made of silver or copper.
Enter the part in which the pomegranate is into the mouth of the patient until the vapor ascends to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula becomes warm, then repeat it several times until it withers, and beware of doing this treatment at the beginning of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling, and it is necessary to do that when the heat of the swelling has subsided. If you do not have this tool, take a reed and attach an eggshell to its end so that the mouth of the patient is not burned.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the vapor from burning the mouth, and this is one of the good treatments with its safety.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On the extraction of thorns and what gets stuck in the throat other than that.
It often happens that a bone or a fish thorn or something else gets stuck in the throat, so it is necessary to extract what is visible to the eye after pressing the tongue with the tool in the sun.
To make clear to you what is in the throat, and what is not visible to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary for the patient to vomit before his food is digested in his stomach, perhaps the stuck thing will come out with the vomiting or the patient will swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root.
Or he swallows a piece of dry bread, or you take a piece of soft sea sponge and tie it with a thread, then he swallows it. When it reaches the location of the thorn, quickly pull the thread, do this several times.
For often the thorn or bone sticks in it and comes out, and if it does not come out with what we have mentioned, otherwise use a leaden tool in this form:
It will be a little thicker than the probe, and at its end there is a hook, the patient inserts it into his throat gently while raising his head upwards, and he is careful not to touch his larynx lest he causes a cough, and he pushes the bone or thorn, or the doctor inserts it with his hand.
The patient's insertion of it is better, for he knows the location of the stuck thing and pushes it downwards or pulls it upwards with the tool, all of that as much as is prepared for him until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On the extraction of the leech stuck in the throat.
If you treat the leech with what we have mentioned in the division of treatment with medicines and it does not work, then look in the patient's throat in the sun after pressing his tongue with the tool I described to you.
If your eye falls on the leech, pull it with a small hook or a tightly closed tweezers, and if you cannot with them, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert into the cavity of the tube an iron heated by fire.
Do that several times, and the patient will endure without water all day, then he takes a basin full of cold water and opens his mouth in it and rinses with it and does not swallow a drop of it and moves the water from time to time with his hand.
For the leech falls on the spot when it senses the water, and if it does not come out with what we have described, then fumigate the throat with urine and with asafoetida with the tool described for the fumigation of the uvula, do that several times, for it will fall.
The way of working in the fumigation is that you take an amount in which there is glowing embers heated by fire, and the container is covered with a lid in the middle of which there is a hole, then you install the end of the tool in that hole, then you throw the incense, and the patient places his mouth in the end of the tube and closes his mouth so that the incense does not come out until he knows that the incense has reached his throat.
For the leech falls on the spot, and if it does not fall, then the incense is repeated several times, and the patient endures the thirst and eats the salty and garlic, and does not drink water, and it must come out with this treatment, and this is the shape of the tool with which the leech is extracted from the throat if it is seen.
And it resembles the claws as you see, except that it has this hook that enters the throat, and its ends resemble the beak of a bird, in which there is the roughness of the file if you grasp something, you will not let go of it.
And another tool with which the tongue is pressed, and it is a wide iron ring that can accommodate the mouth above the tongue, it is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so the mouth remains open without the patient's will, and he cannot remove it at all until you remove it.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth according to this specific measurement.
Chapter Forty.
On the discussion of lancing and incising swellings.
There are many diverse types of swellings, as will be mentioned one by one in this book, and they differ in their lancing and incision in two ways: first in the type of swelling itself and the fluids it contains.
The second type differs according to the locations where they occur in the body, for a swelling occurring in the head is unlike a swelling occurring in the anus, and a swelling occurring in a fleshy area is unlike a swelling occurring in a joint - each one has its own procedural rule.
Among swellings are those that should not be lanced except after the pus has matured and completed, and among them are those that should be lanced while raw and not fully matured, like swellings that are close to joints.
For when a swelling occurs near a joint and its condition persists until what surrounds it becomes infected, it may corrupt the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing permanent disability of that limb, or the swelling may be near a vital organ.
For if you delay its lancing until it matures, you harm that vital organ, or it may be near the anus, so you lance it while raw. The reason for lancing an immature swelling near the anus is to prevent the depth from becoming infected and penetrating into the anus, turning into a fistula.
And becoming incurable. You should know the time to lance fully matured swellings, which is when the swelling's pain subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and throbbing decrease, the top of the swelling becomes wrinkled, and other signs appear.
The incision should be made at the lowest point of the swelling if possible, to make it easier for the pus to flow downward, or at the thinnest and most protruding part of the swelling. The incision should follow the length of the body if the swellings are in areas like the hands and feet.
And in areas of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and generally in all straight areas that do not bend. As for areas that bend, the incision should follow the nature of that area. And if the swelling is in fleshy areas, it is better to leave its lancing.
Until its maturation is complete as we said, for if you lance it before that, the discharge of pus will be prolonged and there will be much dirt and filth, and perhaps its lips and depth will harden. Some swellings must be lanced across the body when necessary or according to what the organ requires.
Small swellings should receive a single incision, and large swellings should receive a wide incision or multiple cuts according to the size of the swelling. There are some swellings where the skin should be hollowed out and cut if it has become like a rag.
And has reached the point of death, as happens in many abscesses and hidden swellings. Some require a triangular incision, and some require cutting in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like groin swellings.
Some require circular incisions and crescent-shaped incisions and similar cuts. Those without a head, like smooth flat swellings, should only receive a simple incision. If the swelling is large and has collected much pus and you lance it, you should not rush to remove all the pus at that time.
Rather, remove some of it, then bind the swelling until another day, then remove some pus again, doing this repeatedly and gradually until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or a very elderly person, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden exit of pus, and the patient might die without you realizing, so be extremely cautious of this matter.
After lancing these swellings, you should wipe the wound and examine it. If the wound of the swelling is small or if there is a single simple incision, use wicks made of linen or old cotton, and if the swelling is large and there are many incision cuts.
You should insert a wick into each incision until they connect with each other, and if part of the tumor has been cut from the skin or hollowed out, you should stuff it with worn cotton or linen fibers without moisture and bind it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your operation, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a linen cloth in them and apply it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the procedures, treatments, and powders that we have described in many places in this book and in the classification. If it is winter and the tumor site has many nerves, you should moisten the dressings with wine and hot oil.
And place them on the site, and if it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply dressings soaked in water and oil or wine and oil, all of this cold, until the third day as we said, then you should untie the tumor and wipe it.
And use in its treatment what suits it until it heals. This is what you need to know about tumor treatment in general, and as for the detailed approach, I have mentioned how to treat each tumor, organized and summarized.
Chapter Forty-One.
On Incising Tumors That Occur in the Scalp.
Small tumors occur in the scalp, and they are types of cysts contained in membranes that are like chicken gizzards, and there are many types: some are fatty, and some contain moisture resembling mud.
And some contain moisture resembling crushed wheat and soup and such, and some are hardened and solid, and there is no danger in incising and removing any of them unless you encounter an artery during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument whose image follows this, which is called the probe, until you know what they contain. If what they contain is fluid, make a simple longitudinal incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from line B to line C, and when the fluid is emptied, peel away the sac that contained that fluid and cut all of it, leaving absolutely nothing of it, for it often returns if any of it remains.
Then dip cotton in Egyptian ointment if available, or else in salt water, and fill the wound with it and leave it until the next day, for it will consume what remains of the sac. Then reapply the cotton with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if you need to do so.
Until you are certain that nothing remains of the sac, then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. And if the tumor contains a fatty cyst, make an incision in this manner:
A cruciform incision, and place hooks in the lips of the wound and peel it from all sides, and strive your utmost to remove the membrane that contains it. If you encounter an artery, do as I described to you later, and likewise do the same with the tumor if it is hardened, regarding the incision.
And the treatment is exactly as I mentioned. And incising the hardened tumor is easier because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these tumors in the head in some people have no moisture at all, for I once made an incision on a tumor in...
The head of an elderly woman and found the tumor like a hard stone, rough and white, that could not be broken, and if someone were to throw it, it would cause a head wound. And as for the rest of the tumors occurring in the head unlike these, such as tumors that occur in children's heads...
And near the bases of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision and make their opening from the bottom to facilitate the flow of matter downward, then treat them with appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-Two.
On Incising Scrofulous Tumors That Occur in the Neck. These tumors frequently occur in the neck and under the armpits and in the groins, and they may occur in the rest of the body. What occurs in the neck may be single or multiple, and some generate from others.
Each of these scrofulous tumors is contained within its own special membrane, as occurs in sebaceous cysts and head tumors as we described. There are many types of these scrofulous tumors - some are hardened, some contain fluids, and some are malignant that don't respond to treatment.
When you find one that feels good to the touch, with its surface close to skin color, moving in all directions, and not attached to the neck nerves, jugular vein, or artery, and not deep-seated,
You should make a simple incision from top to bottom of the body in this shape from line C to line B:
Separate it from all sides and stretch the skin edges with one hook, two hooks, or multiple hooks if needed, as we mentioned for head tumors, and remove it gradually while being careful not to cut any vessel or nerve, and the scalpel should not be too sharp.
Lest your hand slip while cutting or the patient becomes agitated and you cut what need not be cut. If you cut a vessel or artery that hinders your work, place ground vitriol or some of the powder medications that stop bleeding in the wound.
Bind the wound and leave it until the tumor's acute phase subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to suppurate, for the bleeding will stop then. Return to your work until finished, then probe with your index finger if any other small tumors remain, cut them out and clean the area.
If there is a large vessel at the base of the tumor, you should not cut the tumor from its base, but rather tie it with a strong doubled thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm. Then pack the wound with old cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it.
If you remove the entire tumor, you should bring together the wound edges and suture it immediately after ensuring no remnant remains at all. If you see that remnants remain due to the tumor's size,
You should aim to cut at its base and root, and employ suturing as we mentioned. For tumors containing fluids, make a simple incision where you see its ripeness appears, and make the incision toward the bottom as we said.
Then after the incision, use wicks with Egyptian ointment and similar medications to consume what corruption remains until when you see the wound has cleaned, treat it with flesh-growing ointments until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Three
On Incising the Larynx for Swelling that Occurs Inside the Throat
The ancients mentioned this laryngeal incision and I haven't seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the sequence of their words: As for those with quinsy, they should avoid laryngeal incision as they won't benefit from it.
Because all the vessels and lungs are diseased. But for those who have hot swelling in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, when there is no disease in the trachea, laryngeal incision must be used to escape the damage that comes from suffocation.
When we want to do this, we should make a small transverse incision in the larynx below three or four tracheal rings between two rings, just enough that the incision is in the membrane not the cartilage, and this location is suitable for incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and blood vessels are far from it. If the practitioner is timid, he should stretch the throat skin with a hook then incise the skin until reaching the trachea, avoiding blood vessels if he sees any.
Then incise the membrane we described. The incision of the trachea is indicated by the phlegm that comes out with what is pierced and by loss of voice. Leave the wound open for a time, and when the time of feared suffocation has passed, bring together the skin wound edges.
Suture it alone without the cartilage, then use medications that grow flesh until it heals. The author of this book said: The interpretation of all this speech we related is for when they see the patient whose throat is blocked by one of these swellings they mentioned.
When the patient approached death and was about to expire, they proceeded to cut the larynx so the patient could breathe somewhat through the wound site and be saved from death. They ordered that the wound be left open until the severity of the illness passed, and its severity [typically] lasts three days or thereabouts.
At that point, they ordered the wound to be sutured and treated until it healed. What I witnessed myself was that a servant took a knife and directed it at her throat, cutting part of the bronchus. I was called to treat her and found her making sounds like a slaughtered animal, so I examined the wound.
I found that the blood which had emerged from the wound was minimal, so I was certain she hadn't cut an artery or jugular vein, and air was coming out of the wound. I promptly sutured the wound and treated it until it healed. Nothing affected the servant except some hoarseness in her voice, nothing more.
She returned after several days to her best condition, and from this we say that cutting the larynx carries no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On Incising the Swelling That Appears on the External Throat, Called the Elephant of the Throat.
This swelling called the elephant of the throat becomes a large swelling the color of the body, and it is common in women, and it is of two types.
Either it is natural or it is incidental. As for the natural type, there is no treatment for it, and as for the incidental, it comes in two forms: one resembles fatty cysts, and the other form resembles swelling that comes from arterial knotting, and cutting it is dangerous.
One should not approach it with iron [surgical tools] at all, except for those that are small, if you probe it and examine it with the probe and find it resembles a fatty cyst and is not attached to any of the vessels, then cut it as you would cut into cysts.
Extract it with whatever surrounds it of the sac if it is in a sac, and if not, then remove all of it thoroughly, then treat the area with whatever treatment is appropriate.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On Incising the Types of Cysts.
Cysts have many types, and I have mentioned all their types in the Classification, and we should explain here the difference between a cyst and an abscess as it is confusing. I say that an abscess comes with heat and fever and fearsome pains until the boiling of the excess subsides.
And when the rot is complete, then the fever and intensity subside. And the cyst does not come with heat, nor fever, nor pains, and it is contained in a membranous sac which is its special container, and it is the color of the body, and its beginning is like a chickpea and becomes like a melon and larger and smaller.
And it is of two types: either fatty or containing moisture, and the colors of the moisture can be many as mentioned in the Classification. So when you proceed to treat the cyst, you should first probe and examine it with the tool called the probe, whose image will come in the chapter after this.
[Determine] the size of the swelling, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If liquid moisture of any color comes out, then make a simple incision as mentioned regarding other swellings, and if no moisture comes out after the probe, know that it is fatty.
Make a cross-shaped incision on it in this form as I have taught you, and hang it with hooks and carefully separate the skin from all sides, and preserve the sac if you can manage to remove it intact with the cyst.
If the sac tears during the operation and you cannot remove it intact, as often happens, then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains of it, for if anything remains of it, be it little or much, the cyst will return in most cases.
If you are overcome and a small amount remains, sprinkle the wound when you finish with some sharp caustic powders and bind the wound and place above it what will calm the hot swelling and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the cyst was large, suture its edges and treat it with what will cause it to heal.
If you encounter a pulsating or non-pulsating vessel and bleeding occurs, quickly fill the area with crushed vitriol and leave it bound for two or three days until the wound becomes scarred and the blood's boiling subsides, then return to cutting what remains of the cyst.

Chapter Forty-Seven
On the Treatment of Men's Breasts
Which resembles women's breasts - some people's breasts swell at puberty until they resemble women's breasts, remaining swollen and unsightly. For those who dislike this, make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this shape:
From line B to line C:
Then peel away all the fat, then fill the wound with healing medicine, then join the wound lips with stitches and treat until it heals. If the breast droops downward and becomes loose due to its size, as happens with women.

Chapter Forty-Eight
On Lancing Tumors That Occur Under the Armpit
These tumors that occur under the armpit are of the scrofulous type, hard and contained in a sac, and some contain fluids. For those containing fluids, make a crescent-shaped incision in this form:
