Chapter Seventy-Eight
On Extracting the Placenta
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold her breath, then make her sneeze with hellebore and cover her mouth and nostrils with your hand. If it comes out with this, fine; if not, take a pot.
Make a hole in the lid and put in it herbs that open the mouth of the uterus such as pennyroyal, rue, dill, chamomile, wormwood, cassia, and centaury - all these medicines or some of them - cover them with water and put them over fire, then place a silver tube over the hole in the pot's lid with the other end in the mouth of the uterus.
Hold it until the steam reaches the depth of the uterus, then make her sneeze as we said, and the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and doesn't come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in sesame oil or marshmallow mucilage.
Then insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta, and when she finds it, grasp it and pull it little by little until it comes out. If it is attached deep in the uterus, insert the hand as we described until when you find the placenta, pull it gently little by little with care.
So that the uterus does not fall during strong pulling, rather it should be gently moved to the sides, right and left, then increase the amount of pulling, for it will respond then and become free from adhesion. If the mouth of the uterus is closed, we have described the treatment with sneezing and the treatment with pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, then beware of being violent with it in repeating the pulling, but rather what has emerged from it should be tied to the mouth of the uterus to the woman's thigh, then inject it with the quadruple ointment, for it will cause it to decay after days.
And it dissolves and comes out, except that when it decays, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and this harms the patient, so appropriate fumigation should be used.
For that, some of the ancients have tried fumigation with watercress and dried figs. The form of the device with which the woman is fumigated for retention of menstruation and placenta and such:
It is made of lead resembling a funnel or made of copper, the thin end is placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire and incense placed on embers, hold it until that incense is gone and replace it with another.
Chapter Seventy-Nine
On the treatment of the imperforate anus.
Many children are born with their anuses imperforate, sealed by a thin membrane, so the midwife should pierce that membrane with her finger, or else incise it with a sharp lancet and be careful not to touch the muscle.
Then place on it wool soaked in wine and oil, then treat it with ointments until it heals. If you fear it might seal again, place a lead tube in the opening for many days and remove it when the child needs to defecate.
Anal closure may also occur from the healing of a wound or swelling, so that scar should be cut then treated with what we mentioned of treatment and lead.
Chapter Eighty
On the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower parts.
Fistulas that occur in the lower parts are nodules and thickening that occur near the anus from outside or in the space from one of the sides, and the fistula may be one or more.
When that nodule becomes chronic, it opens and discharges watery white moisture or thin pus. Some of these fistulas may penetrate to the passage of feces and to the intestine, and some may be non-penetrating. The penetrating ones can be recognized by what comes out of them of feces and wind when the patient defecates.
Sometimes worms come out of them, and some fistulas, when they are in the space, may penetrate to the bladder and to the urethral passage, and some may penetrate to the hip joint and to the tail bone. And how to recognize a fistula that penetrates to the anus from a non-penetrating one:
Insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe into the fistula made of copper or iron if there is no tortuosity in the fistula. If there is tortuosity in it, insert a thin lead probe or a horse hair.
Until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger. If you don't feel it at all and nothing comes out of the hole of feces, wind, or worms as we said, then know it is non-penetrating and proceed with treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urinary passage, its indication is the discharge of urine from it and its refusal to heal with medicines. As for if it penetrates to the hip joint or to the tail bone, its sign is the probe reaching there.
If there is no tortuosity to the bone, and finding pain in that direction, and constant discharge of pus from it, and that no treatment is effective and it doesn't heal with ointment - all these penetrating fistulas have absolutely no cure.
Their treatment is futile effort for those ignorant physicians who dare to treat them. As for those that are non-penetrating and not chronic, cure can be hoped for with iron [surgical intervention] as I will describe and as I have tried, which is to lay the patient before you on his back.
And raise his legs upward with his thighs inclined toward his belly, then insert a probe of copper or lead if there is curvature in the fistula until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the anus, you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe with your finger having penetrated by itself, exposed without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then know with certainty that it is penetrating and do not tire yourself with it as there is no cure for it as we said.
They have said that it heals in some people rarely, and among the treatments from which benefit is hoped is to heat a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula as previously mentioned and insert it hot into the fistula until it reaches near the anus, then repeat it two or three times.
Until you know that all that excess compacted flesh which resembles a bird's quill tube has been burned, then treat it with wicks soaked in ghee until that burned flesh comes out, then treat it with flesh-growing ointments if it heals.
And if not, it will never heal with any other treatment. And if you inserted the probe and it did not penetrate to your finger in the anus, and there was a thick barrier of flesh or membrane between them, and you saw the fistula near the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula.
And you continue with the incision along with the probe while it is in the fistula until you reach with the incision where the tip of the probe ends and the probe becomes free and falls, then clean out all that compacted flesh that resembles a bird's quill tube and don't leave any of it.
Then treat it with flesh-growing ointments until it heals. If blood overcomes you and prevents you from your work due to cutting that flesh, then the best thing to do and the quickest benefit is to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine because cauterization with fire combines two good conditions: burning that excess flesh.
And stopping the blood and drying the moistures, then treat it with wicks soaked in ghee or sulfur ground with oil until the area suppurates and that burned flesh comes out in the pus, then treat the wound at that time with flesh-growing ointments that grow hard flesh.
These are the medicines that have astringency and dry, for when it heals it has been cured and does not return. If the fistula has reached the depth of the anus and is far from the body's surface, insert your finger into the anus and examine with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it is a barrier of membrane or flesh, and it was close to penetrating, then the work on it is only by way of ambition and hope, and that is to use one of three approaches: either to cauterize it as we said.
Or to cut it until you reach its bottom so you can manage to insert wicks and treatment from nearby, and do not persist with the cutting lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus and cause involuntary defecation for the patient, then treat it with what we mentioned and perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or to penetrate that barrier and pierce it after cutting it near the anus as we said, either with the probe or with another sharp-tipped instrument, then clean all that compacted flesh that is in the cut to where you can thoroughly.
Then attempt to heal the entire wound with the cut and the upper opening until it seals and leave the fistula open inside the anus as it will be easier on the patient, and the fistula may perforate in this manner.
And that is when you insert the probe into the fistula and it was on a side of the anus near the body's surface with the skin and the ring of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a cobbler's needle of this shape:
And insert into it a twisted thread of five threads or so, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom, if it has penetrated into the edge of the anus from inside nearby.
Pull out the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the rectum and extract the end of the thread, then gather both ends together and tie them, leaving them for a day or two. Whenever the thread cuts through the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the thread ends is cut and falls off, then treat the wound until it heals.
If the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate however possible for you, unless it is very deep, in which case you should not do this because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then proceed with it as we mentioned until it heals.
Image of the thorny scalpel used to cut fistulas - its hook should be very sharp while the other side should not be sharp, lest you cut what does not need cutting, as you see:
Chapter Eighty-One
On piercing bleeding hemorrhoids, cutting them, and treating fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red blisters or like grape seeds, and they can be small or large, with blood flowing from them constantly.
They can be single or multiple, and they can be outside the rectum and at its edges, though these external ones are usually less moist, releasing yellow fluid or a small amount of blood continuously, and are the same color as the body.
The treatment for internal hemorrhoids is to order the patient to defecate and strain until the rectum protrudes and the warts appear to you, then quickly grab them with hooks or hold them with your nail and cut them at their roots. If the hooks don't hold due to their moisture and laxity,
Scrape them with a rough cloth or a piece of goat hair fabric, or pull them with your fingers then cut them, then after cutting sprinkle on them some sharp powders to serve as cauterization, or cauterize them as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat them with the rest of the treatment until healed. If the rectum doesn't respond to coming out, give the patient an enema with slight irritation to wash what's in the rectum and make it come out quickly when the patient strains. As for the external warts outside the rectum, their matter is easy and simple.
It is to take them with your nail or catch them with a hook and cut them, then treat them as we mentioned until they heal. For those who dislike cutting with iron, they should pierce them in this manner: take a twisted thread and put it through a needle.
Then pull the wart upward and pierce it with the needle at its base from the other side, and wrap the thread ends below the needle while it's crossing, and tie the wart tightly, then knot the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all warts but leave one unpierced.
To allow blood waste to flow from it, then place on the rectum a cloth soaked in rose oil or ceratum and place it on the rectum, and order the patient to rest, then leave it until they fall off. When the warts fall off, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
As for treating fissures, they often occur from dry stool and constipation. If chronic and no medicine helps, you should scrape it with a scalpel blade or your nail until it becomes moist and the upper crust that prevents healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it doesn't heal, repeat with stronger scraping than the first time until it bleeds well and peels, then treat it and it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-Two
On treating inverted and non-inverted corns, dry warts, and anthill-like growths.
A corn is a round nodule the color of the body resembling a nail head, occurring throughout the body but especially on the soles of the feet and toes, causing pain when walking.
One should cut around the corn and grasp it with forceps or a hook and remove it from its root, then treat it, and if you wish, cauterize it as previously mentioned in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, compacted, round protrusion above the body's surface.
Warts often occur on children's hands. The treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it or pierce it with a silk thread or hair until it falls off. If you wish, you can cauterize it as previously mentioned with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Inverted and non-inverted warts can be removed with the instrument that I will mention in the treatment of formication. As for formication, it is also a small, hardened, thick protrusion on the body's surface that goes very deep, and when cold affects it, one feels something similar to ant bites.
It can occur anywhere on the body but most commonly occurs on the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube from a goose feather or a strong eagle feather and place that tube over the formication until the circle of the tube surrounds it from all sides, then rotate your hand with the tube until you cut around the formication.
Make it go deep into the flesh and remove the formication from its root. If you wish, you can do this with a copper or iron tube of this shape:
The top of the tube should be solid and twisted toward thinness to make it easy for the fingers to grip and twist it. If you wish, you can cauterize the formication as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization of warts.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the shapes of instruments used for enemas in treating rectal conditions, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe can be made from silver, porcelain, or cast or hammered copper, and these instruments can be made in small and large sizes according to their users.
Those used for treating small children should be small, and for those with narrow or painful rectums, their syringes should be very fine. Here is the shape of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel where the leather bag is tied should be wide in this manner, with a barrier where the bag is tied as you see, and its lower end that enters the rectum should be solid, very smooth, tapering to thinness, with two holes on one side and one hole on the other side.
The width of the hole should be the thickness of a probe or slightly thicker, and the leather bag in which medicines are placed should be made from an animal bladder or sheep skin, made in the shape of a small dining mat with a diameter of one span.
Many holes should be pierced around it in a circle, with the distance between each hole being a finger's width. Then insert a strong thread made of yarn twisted from ten threads or so through these holes and gather the leather bag like a dining mat.
When medicine is placed in it, it should be tied tightly at the head of the syringe between the two stones, then used for injection. This is the shape of the circular leather bag and holes:
The method of using it for enema is to fill the skin or bladder with medicine that has been warmed to the patient's tolerance - be careful that it's neither too hot nor too cold - then have the patient lie on their back and raise their legs up.
Place a leather mat or sheet under them, then lubricate the tip of the syringe with oil, egg white, fenugreek mucilage, or similar substances, then insert it gently into the rectum. Then an assistant should take the leather bag with both hands and squeeze it firmly until all the medicine in the syringe empties into the intestine.
Then remove the syringe and have the patient raise their legs against the wall. If the medicine urges them to evacuate, they should hold it as long as possible, and if they sleep with it all night, that would be most beneficial. If it was injected for diarrheal conditions and comes out quickly, repeat the enema until they recover.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful swelling in the rectum, the syringe should be very fine and smooth, like those used for children. This is the shape of a fine syringe with its leather attached:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
I have mentioned in the classification the treatment of what resembles cauterizations, and I will mention here their complete treatment by hand and medicines. I say that wounds differ according to what causes the wound and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things that cause wounds are many, such as being struck by a stone, cut by a sword or knife, pierced by a spear or stick or arrow, and many other such things.
As for wounds according to body locations, they are like wounds that occur on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and similar organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a standard and rule for other wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds in particular because compound wounds will be mentioned in the first section of the third chapter of this book.
I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no bone fracture, I examine it. If it was from the strike of a stone or similar, and it only bruised the skin, and the wound was large, and you fear the occurrence of hot swelling in the patient, then proceed immediately to perform venesection of the cephalic vein.
Do not delay this and draw blood according to his strength if there is no impediment to bloodletting, especially if the blood coming from the wound is minimal, and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drink, and thick foods. If there is an impediment to bloodletting, then let him reduce food intake and avoid meats and drinks, and apply to the wound, if hot swelling occurs, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with astringent wine.
If you are safe from hot swelling, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh with its blood and the air has not altered it, the powder which I will describe to you shortly. But if the air has altered it, apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and was from a sword cut or similar, and its lips do not come together with bandages, then join it with sutures as I will describe in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and is hanging, with only a small attachment remaining, then cut it off and treat the wound with medicines that will generate firm flesh in place of the skin, and order the patient to nourish himself with foods that have substance like heads and harisa and similar items.
If decay occurs in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, cut off all the decayed part and discard it, then treat it. If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding and the blood does not stop with medicines, then search for the artery.
If you find it and it's not completely severed, then sever it with the lancet and tie it, and if necessity demands when what we mentioned doesn't help, then cauterize it until the blood stops. If these wounds are small and simple, their matter is easy and straightforward, sufficient to treat them by sprinkling this powder on them while they have their blood.
Before the air alters them, the recipe for the powder is to take one part frankincense, two parts dragon's blood, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime, grind all together and sift, and fill the wound with it and bind it well until it adheres to it properly.
And it congeals with the blood, then bind it from above with bandages and leave it without loosening it as long as it doesn't swell, for the medicine will not detach from it until it heals. And lime alone can do this if frankincense and dragon's blood are not available to you.
This also has the same effect in many large wounds when its application and binding are done properly, and the wounds are fresh with their blood and the air has not altered them. But if the air has altered them somewhat, especially if it is in summer time.
Then there is no point in applying this powder, so treat it as we mentioned by applying some ripening ointments or apply to it a porridge made from barley flour made with water and honey until pus forms, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
As for if a bone fracture occurs with the wound and it was minor, then pull it with forceps. I have mentioned the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in the section on neck wounds. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a head wound if it is simple.
But if it has cut a nerve from the neck or an artery, if it is a nerve, there is no solution except to avoid treating it with strongly astringent substances like verdigris and vitriol and similar things because they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
And nothing cold should be placed on the wound at all because the substance of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs, and what you treat it with should be from soft medicines like lime washed with fresh water multiple times, moistened with oil or rose oil and tutty.
If also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner and moist ointments, and if the wound is large, use stitching or press its edges together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has depth and there is a cavity at its bottom where pus has collected, then lance it at its lowest point, and if an artery has been severed in the wound and you find its blood flowing, then cut it or tie it or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
If the wound has cut some or all of the throat cartilage rings but the jugular veins are intact, then join the skin edges with stitches over the windpipe but do not touch the windpipe itself; rather, align it and return it to its natural shape, then bind it firmly and leave it for days.
Then treat it with astringent and drying ointments until it heals, and if the wound has affected the bone, examine it, and if there are bone fragments, extract all of them or as many as possible, and what you cannot remove, leave it until the wound suppurates and heals and its removal becomes easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle upon it the powder we described, and if bone in the wound has become necrotic and turned into a fistula, treat it as will be mentioned in its chapter, in wounds of the chest and between the shoulder blades.
If it is a stab from a spear or knife and you see it has depth, observe if air comes out of it when the patient breathes, then know it is a fatal wound, but if it has no depth and is fresh with blood, do not apply powder to it immediately nor bind it lest blood becomes trapped in its depth.
The blood might then flow back to the heart and kill the patient, but rather apply a drawing ointment and loosen it twice during the day, and if you don't have ointment available, place old cotton in the wound's opening to absorb what comes out of it in terms of fluids, and have the patient sleep on the wound.
So what collects in it may drain, and if three days or more have passed since the wound occurred and the patient has not developed spasms, nor bad palpitations, nor difficulty breathing, and you see all other conditions are good, know that the wound is safe and treat it with wicks and other treatments until it heals.
If healing becomes difficult and pus continues to flow constantly, know that it has become a fistula and treat it according to its chapter, [have] the patient [lie] on the wound so what collects in it may drain, and if the chest or back wound has persisted, treat it with the previously mentioned stitching.
If it is large, or with powder if it is small, and if it has affected the bone and fragments have been cut from it, examine the wound and hasten to remove those fragments if they are loose, and if they are not loose, leave them until.
The wound suppurates as their removal will become easier, and as for the rest of the wounds occurring in other body parts, their rule in treatment is the rule of what we mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Five.
On abdominal wounds, intestinal protrusion, and their suturing.
The tear that occurs in the abdomen can be large, small, or medium-sized. From the large one, an intestine or several intestines may protrude, making their insertion and suturing more difficult and challenging.
The small tear is also difficult in another way, as returning [the intestine] becomes harder, and if you don't hasten to return the intestine to its place immediately, it will become swollen and thick, making its insertion difficult. Therefore, the medium-sized tear became the most favorable, as returning the intestine is not as difficult as with these two types.
Know that abdominal suturing has four methods: two general sutures that are suitable for both abdominal suturing and suturing other body wounds, and two other specific sutures that while usable for all wounds, are more specific to abdominal suturing.
I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and something of the intestine protrudes and is difficult to return, this is due to one of two reasons: either the smallness of the tear as we said, or because the intestine has become swollen from exposure to cold air.
If this is the case, you should warm it by dipping a sponge or wet cloth in lukewarm water alone or water in which lemon grass, sedge, and spikenard have been boiled, and sprinkle it on the intestine until the swelling subsides.
Wine with some astringency can also do this, and it is better than water alone in reducing swelling. When you see the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow or mallow has been boiled, as this will ease its entry with minimal effort.
If its return remains difficult after this treatment, make a small incision in the tear with the instrument used for cutting fistulas. The first treatment, when possible, is better than cutting, and cutting is only resorted to out of necessity and when other methods fail. This is the shape of the instrument:
One of its ends is curved and sharp, and the other end is not sharp. The thin end is not as thin as a lancet but slightly flatter, and it is an instrument that resembles a small polo stick, as you can see. When the wound widens and the intestine enters, it should be returned to its natural shape.
And its specific place if you can manage that is best. If the tear is wide and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs higher than their head, and if it is in the upper abdomen, their head and chest should be higher than their lower body.
Similarly, if the tear is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to keep the side with the wound higher than the other side. This should be done for both major wounds and medium-sized wounds.
As for small wounds, position them as is possible for you. Then have a gentle assistant present who holds the entire tear with their hands and brings its edges together, then reveals it bit by bit to the person doing the suturing.
This is the description of one general suture: take one or several needles according to the width of the wound, then leave the width of a little finger from the edge of the tear and insert one needle without thread through the edges of the skin.
Along with the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin from the inside until you pass it through from that side, having gathered the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers. Then tie with a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the wound edges come together firmly.
Then leave the width of a finger again and insert another needle, then secure it with thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as needed until you complete closing the entire wound, and let the needles be medium between thick and thin.
Because very thin needles quickly cut through flesh and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin, therefore they should be medium in both thinness and thickness, and the needle should not be inserted very close to the edge of the skin.
Lest the flesh quickly breaks and the wound opens before healing, and also do not distance the stitching too far lest the wound fails to heal, then cut the ends of the needles so they don't harm the patient during sleep and place cotton pads on each side to hold the needle ends.
Leave them until you know that the wound has healed, and this type of needle stitching is thus more suitable for small wounds because their stitching can be sufficient with one or two needles or so.
As for the description of the second common suturing, it is to join the four edges together with stitching - I mean the two edges of the skin and the two edges of the peritoneum - at once with a needle threaded with moderately thin and thick twisted thread, then when you have passed through these four edges with the needle, return the needle from the same side you started from.
So the thread forms a lattice from above the wound, making the stitching similar to the stitching of bags used to tie goods, and make between each stitch a distance of the width of the little finger, and this type of stitching is known to all people.
With this stitching I treated a wound that occurred to a man in his abdomen who had been wounded with a knife, and the tear of the wound was more than a span, and about two spans of the middle intestine had protruded, and the tear was in the middle of the abdomen.
I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours, and the wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he recovered and lived many years after that, going about all his affairs, though the physicians had judged that he would never recover.
Remarkably, I did not treat him with ointment because I was in a place where no medicines were available, so I would place old cotton on the wound twice a day and attend to washing it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of specialized stitching, I mention it according to Galen's exact words, which is to begin the stitching from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside, and when you have passed the needle through the skin and through the muscle that runs straight along the length of the entire abdomen.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside in the other edge of the thin part (of the abdomen), and when you have passed it, pass it again in this same edge of the thin part from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum which is on this side and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and pass it through with your passing through the peritoneum to the edge of the thin part which is on its side until it passes through all of it.
Then begin again from this same side and stitch it with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side and bring out the needle from the skin near it, then return it in that skin and stitch the edge of the peritoneum which is on the other side with this edge of the thin part.
And bring it out from the skin which is on its side, and do this once more and do it time after time until you stitch the entire wound in this manner, and the complete description of this stitching is that you stitch like the furriers' stitching of fur.
By stitching the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and leaving it once from the other side until you finish, and as for the second type of specialized stitching which Galen also mentioned, these are his exact words.
It involves stitching it as some healers do by joining each part to its naturally corresponding counterpart, connecting the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the abdominal wall to its other edge, and this is done as I will describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the edge of the abdominal wall closest to you from the outside and pass it through to the inside alone, leaving the edges of the peritoneum, then return the needle and pass it from outside to inside through both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And pass it from inside to outside through the other edge of the abdominal wall on the opposite side, and this type of suturing is better than the common simple suturing which was mentioned before. This is Galen's text verbatim, and some have said that abdominal suturing can be done with two needles.
This is done by threading one thread through them and beginning by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side, then inserting the other needle from the opposite side and passing it through from your side, just like the cobbler's stitch.
Know that when the tear is in the middle of the abdomen, its suturing is more difficult than other areas of the abdomen. As for treating the wound, its treatment follows the same rules as other wounds, such that when you encounter a fresh wound with blood before the air changes it, return the intestine, suture it properly, and apply the adhesive powder.
If the air has altered the wound, apply some of the ointments you have available until it suppurates and the threads fall out, and the peritoneum and abdominal wall heal together, then treat it as you would treat other wounds until it heals.
If you don't have medicines available, apply worn cotton from the beginning of suppuration and change it twice a day as I taught you until it heals, for you won't need another treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound may share pain with the vital organs,
You should soak soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpit. If there is pain or decay in the intestine, which often occurs, inject it with warm black astringent wine.
Especially if the decay has reached deep into the intestine and become a wound penetrating its cavity. Know that the thick intestines are easier to heal, and the thin ones are more difficult to heal.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it never accepts healing from any wound that occurs in it due to the abundance and size of its vessels, the thinness of its substance, and its proximity to the nature of nerves. If what protrudes from the wound is omentum and you encounter it fresh, return it just as you would return the intestine.
If some time has passed and it has turned green or black, you should tie it with a thread above the blackened area to prevent bleeding, as there are vessels and small arteries in the omentum, then cut what is below that ligature.
And make the ends of the thread hang from below the wound outside it to make it easier for you to remove and extract it when the omentum falls off and the wound suppurates. Regarding the wound that occurs in the intestine, if a small tear occurs in the intestine,
It may heal in some people because I saw a person who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach. The wound became chronic and turned into a fistula from which feces and wind would exit. I began treating him although I did not expect him to heal.
I continued to treat him gently until he healed and the area sealed. When I saw that the area had healed, I feared that something bad might happen to the patient in his abdomen, but nothing bad happened to him at all and he remained in the best of conditions.
He remains healthy, eating, drinking, having relations, entering the bath, and exercising in his service. Some experienced practitioners have mentioned that when a small wound occurs in the intestine, it should be sutured in this manner.
Large-headed ants should be taken, then the wound lips brought together, then one ant with its mouth open should be placed on the wound edges. When it grips and closes its mouth, its head should be cut off as it will stick and not detach. Then another ant should be placed near the first one.
Continue doing this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then return it and suture the wound. These heads will remain attached to the intestine until it heals and bonds, causing no harm to the patient whatsoever. The intestine can also be sutured with fine thread.
Which is pulled from the animal's intestinal membrane after being threaded through a needle. The end of this thread from the membrane should be gently pulled, then tied to a thin twisted cotton thread, then this thread should be inserted into the needle.
With the intestinal thread, the intestine is sutured then returned to the cavity. This type of suturing with ants and intestinal membrane is done as a matter of hope and expectation.
However, if the tear is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no solution and no cure whatsoever.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On the Treatment of Sinus and Fistula.
Know that any wound or swelling that becomes chronic and develops into an ulcer that doesn't heal, and continuously discharges pus without stopping, is generally called a fistula in whichever organ it occurs, and we call it a sinus, and the fistula.
It is in reality a hard, white, matted mass without pain, having a cavity like that of a bird's feather.
That is why some call it a quill. Sometimes it is moist, continuously discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture stops at times. This moisture can be abundant or minimal, thick or thin.
I have previously mentioned in the classification when discussing wounds that any ulcer that doesn't heal and doesn't grow flesh is due to one of nine causes: either due to lack of blood in the body, or its poor quality, or because inside it and on its edges there is hard flesh preventing the growth of good flesh.
Or because it is very dirty and unclean, or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter feeding it is of poor quality, or because the medicine is not suitable for its treatment, or due to corruption occurring in the country of an epidemic nature, or due to a peculiarity in the country.
As happened in the city of Saragossa where diseases are difficult to mature and swellings are naturally slow to heal, or because it contains one bone or several bones. I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their medicinal treatments in the classification.
Here I should inform you about the treatment of fistula and sinus that is done through manual operation. Know that fistulas can occur in all body organs: some fistulas extend to great vessels and arteries, or to nerves.
Or to membrane, or to intestine, or to bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the complexly constructed joints like the hand and foot, and some lead near to a vital organ and such.
Whatever fistulas are of this nature are among the diseases difficult to cure that do not accept treatment, and working on them is futility and ignorance. If there are fistulas that have not reached these locations I mentioned, and you hope and expect to treat them.
And you want to investigate this, take a probe of copper or iron if the fistula runs straight and examine it with it. If the fistula has a curve, examine it with a thin lead probe because lead is soft in body.
It becomes easier upon entry and bends towards the curve. If the fistula has many openings that you cannot probe with the instrument, inject one of its openings, for the moisture you inject will flow towards the other openings and discharge from them.
Then thoroughly investigate by whatever means possible to know if there is bone or nerve involved, or if the fistula's bottom is far or near, or if it is a single fistula with multiple openings. Determine all of this to the best of your ability by questioning the patient and noting the pain when you press your hand on the area.
And similar such signs and causes leading to swelling. When you have ascertained all of this with certainty, then proceed to treatment with confidence. Look to see if
The fistula is superficial and nearby, or in a safe location far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places I mentioned to you. Then cut into the fistula as I described previously and remove what is inside it of hardened matter, corrupt flesh, and excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its opening of flesh or warts and the like, and treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep-bottomed and straight, you should cut into its depth as much as possible, then clean it of all its corrupt flesh.
Then use wicks soaked in caustic medicines and insert them to the bottom of the fistula that you can reach with the instrument. Do this several times until the caustic medicine consumes all the corruption remaining at the bottom of the fistula.
Then heal it with flesh-growing ointments until it heals. If it does not heal with this, then cauterize it as described previously. If the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, examine it and cut into it as I told you, if nothing prevents you such as
A vessel or nerve or vital organ as we said. If the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, scrape it until the corruption is gone, then treat it with what will heal it until it heals. If it does not heal and continues to discharge pus as it did before,
Know that you have not reached all of the corruption to remove it. Uncover everything on it again and thoroughly scrape and clean it to the best of your ability, then heal it. If it heals, good; if not, know that the corruption is in a deep location of decay, so uncover everything on it again.
And thoroughly scrape and clean it to the best of your ability. If the corrupt bone is small and you can pull it out, pull it with the fine forceps suitable for that.
If there are many bones, thoroughly extract them all and do not leave any if you can help it. If you encounter any ligaments beneath them and there is no danger in cutting them, cut those ligaments and flesh if present there and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones immediately, apply something to rot it to the wound and leave it for days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones rot. Keep the wound from healing over the incision you made during your operation.
Rather, if you fear this, place upon it cotton soaked in ground sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments, for when those ligaments rot and the bones become friable, their extraction and removal becomes easy.
If it is a single large bone like the shin bone or thigh bone or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption is gone, then heal the wound. If a large part of it is corrupted and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, it must be sawed.
And cut all of it to where the corruption ends, then treat it until it heals. And I will tell you about a case of infection that occurred in a man's leg to make it an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young in age.
About thirty years ago, he experienced pain in his leg due to something moving inside his body until the substances reached the leg and it swelled significantly, without any external cause, then time passed with doctors' mistakes until the swelling opened.
A lot of material discharged from it, and its treatment was mishandled until the leg became infected and developed many openings, all of which were discharging pus and bodily fluids. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them was skilled in surgery.
Until he came to me, and I saw his leg with materials flowing greatly from these openings, and the man had become thin and his color had turned yellow. I inserted the probe into one of these openings, and it led to the bone, then I examined all the openings.
I found them connecting to each other from all sides of the leg, so I hastened to cut into one of these openings until I exposed some of the bone and found it corrupted, eaten away, blackened, rotted, and perforated until the holes reached the marrow, so I sawed what was exposed to me.
I managed to reach the corrupted bone while thinking there was no corruption in the bone other than what I had cut and sawed, and that I had completely removed it. Then I tried to heal the wound with flesh-growing medicines for a longer period, but it didn't heal. Then I returned to expose the bone again above the first exposure.
I found the corruption still connected to the bone, so I sawed what appeared to me of that corruption as well. Then I attempted to heal it, but it neither healed nor closed. Then I exposed it again, and I continued to cut the bone piece by piece and attempt to heal it, but it wouldn't heal, until I had cut about a span of the bone.
I removed it with its marrow, then treated it with medicines, and it healed quickly and recovered. This repetition in the operation and cutting was necessary due to the patient's weakness, low tolerance, and my fear of his death, as he would frequently faint from excessive discharge, but he recovered completely.
Tough flesh grew in place of the bone, his body's condition improved, his strength returned, and he went about his affairs without any walking impairment affecting him at all. If there is a protruding bone in any part of the body that has broken...
You should saw it in this manner: take a bandage and tie it to the end of the protruding bone and order someone to pull it upward, and place another bandage of wool thicker than the first bandage.
Then tie it to the flesh below the bone and pull its end so the flesh is drawn downward while you expose the flesh from the area you want to saw, so the saw doesn't harm the flesh, and place a piece of wood or board under the bone from below firmly.
Because if you do this, the saw won't be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone, and the sawing should be slightly above the site of corruption so that if there is corruption in the bone's cavity that isn't apparent on its surface to the senses, you won't need to saw it again.
If the bone is corrupted but not protruding, rather connected to itself with corruption in its middle or in part of it, expose the flesh from all its sides, then place the wood from below, then saw it from the first side where the corruption is.
When you finish sawing it, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the corruption as we said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection between two joints, there is no solution other than scraping. If the corruption is in the metacarpals of the hand or metatarsals of the foot, treating it is very difficult, but you should scrape off the corruption however it appears to you and clean it thoroughly in whatever way possible.
By whatever means available to you when not encountering a vessel or nerve. Know that there are many cutters and saws for cutting these bones according to the position of the bones, their alignment, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and porosity.
Therefore, you should prepare for each type of operation an instrument suitable for that operation.
Know that the operations themselves will guide you to the type of instrument you need when you have long experience and knowledge of the various aspects of this craft, for whoever masters the craft and witnesses various types of diseases.
One devises for oneself suitable instruments for each disease, and I will illustrate for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.
Make them examples to follow and measurements to compare with others, God willing. Image of a saw:

Chapter Eighty-Seven
On Amputating Limbs and Sawing Bones
Extremities may become gangrenous either from external causes or internal causes. If you have treated that corruption with medication treatment and it was not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb with nothing stopping it.
Then you should amputate that limb up to where the corruption has reached so that the patient may be saved from death or from great affliction which is worse than losing the limb. And the sign of one who has this condition is that the limb becomes black until you think fire has burned it.
Or it rots after blackening until that rot spreads to what's adjacent to that limb and begins to affect the whole body, so hasten to cut it. And likewise if the cause of corruption is from the sting of some venomous creatures like sea scorpions or snakes or spiders and such.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and don't allow the corruption to spread to the hand, and likewise if it has taken hold in the hand, cut it from the base of the wrist and don't allow the corruption to spread until it affects the two bones of the forearm.
And likewise if it has taken hold in the forearm, cut the arm at the elbow at the joint itself. If the corruption has passed and you see it spreading toward the shoulder, do not cut at the shoulder at all for in that is the patient's death, and use other treatments as much as possible.
And likewise do with the leg when corruption takes hold in a toe - cut the toe at one of the phalanges, and if it takes hold in the metatarsals cut the entire foot, and if it rises to the knee cut the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee there is no solution except leaving it and surrendering the patient to death. The procedure for cutting or sawing the limb is to tie a bandage below the location you want to cut and tie another bandage above the location.
An assistant pulls one bandage downward and another assistant pulls the upper bandage upward, and you strip away the flesh between the bandages with a wide lancet until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or saw, and you should place cotton cloths from all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy area causing the patient excessive pain and hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your operation, quickly cauterize the area or apply some blood-stopping powders then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will tell you of an example of a man who had this very condition in his leg that I am describing to you, as blackness occurred in his leg with a burning like fire.
This corruption first appeared in his toe until it took over the entire leg. The man hastened on his own, when he saw the corruption spreading in the limb along with the severity of pain and burning he was experiencing, so he cut it at the joint and recovered.
After a long time passed, that same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand. He came to me, and I attempted to repel that excess with medicines I applied to the hand after cleansing his body, but the excess did not repel and began to spread to the other finger.
Until the corruption took over the hand, and he called upon me to amputate his hand, but I refused, hoping to repel that excess. I also feared death for him upon cutting his hand because the man's strength was failing. When he despaired of my help, he returned to his town.
I later learned that he hastened to cut off his entire hand and recovered. I have told this story to serve as an aid regarding what occurs of this type of disease and to be a guide to be guided by and worked upon.
Chapter Eighty-Eight
On the treatment of cavities and how to inject them with medicines.
When a swelling occurs in some fleshy parts and the duration of the swelling extends until pus collects, then it bursts or is lanced and all the pus within it exits, leaving the area empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a rag that has thinned, but the corruption has not reached the point of affecting bone, nerve, or ligament, and from here it deserves to be called a cavity and is not called a fistula unless its duration extends until the corruption affects something of these parts.
At that point it is called a fistula or sinus, and its treatment is to cut all that skin in a circle, especially if it has thinned and become like a rag, and you are certain it will not adhere to the area due to its corruption.
If you hope the skin will adhere and the corruption has not reached that extent, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, treat it with injection. Look at the cavity - if it is large and the pus that extends from it has a foul smell, inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris in equal parts, combined in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and becomes the consistency of honey. Then take what you need of it, thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the cavity, bind its opening, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then extract it by squeezing - do this for days until the cavity is cleaned and the foul smell disappears. It may be injected with ash water if this medicine is not available to you, which is to take ash from grapevine wood or oak wood and pour water on it.
Then strain it and inject with it until you are certain that the cavity has been washed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject it with water and honey mixed with some ground verdigris.
Or inject it with honey and wine mixed, because it is the nature of honey to clean and wash, and wine causes the cavity to adhere, especially if the wine has some astringency and dryness. When you have done this several times and are certain that the cavity's corruption has gone, inject it with what will grow flesh in it.
Such as taking from the palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and astringent wine, or inject it with some of the other medicines and ointments whose descriptions we have established in the treatise on ointments. If the opening of the cavity is too narrow to fit the syringe tube.
Then widen it slightly with iron or place in it a wick smeared with Egyptian ointment or lead ointment until it widens, and likewise if the opening is too wide, bring its lips together with stitching and leave only enough space for the syringe to enter, no more.
And likewise, if the opening from which pus flows is elevated upwards, make an incision at its lowest point so the pus flows downward, because when pus is trapped in the depths of the cavity, it prevents flesh from growing in it.
If you cannot incise the cavity towards its bottom as desired, try to position the limb in such a way that pus flows easily according to what's possible for you, and ensure that no pus whatsoever is retained in it.
As for the flesh-growing medications that are placed on the cavity externally and how to apply them: take one of the appropriate flesh-growing ointments and spread it on a linen cloth, and let the cloth be large enough to cover the entire cavity.
Then cut with scissors opposite the cavity's opening a hole slightly wider than the cavity's mouth, then spread some of the soft ointments on another cloth sized to the hole and place it distanced from the cavity's opening to facilitate pus drainage, and do not remove the large cloth except after.
Several days, but as for the cloth on the cavity's opening, that is what you remove each time to let out accumulated pus and to know what discharge flows from it - whether it is abundant or scant, mature or immature.
Additionally, examine the cavity site itself - whether the patient feels pain in it or not, and whether there is swelling or not. If you continue treatment thus and see that the material emerging from the cavity opening is minimal.
Know that the cavity has adhered or is close to adhering, so then unwrap it tomorrow or on the third day, and when you unwrap the wound, replace the cloths with others and renew the ointment. If thin discharge comes from the cavity after a long period, do not despair of its adhesion.
Its healing is near, because among people some are slow in growing flesh in their wounds while others are the opposite. If immature pus comes from the cavity after many days, know that the cavity has not adhered. If the cavity's adhesion is delayed.
And its condition prolonged, increase the drying medications, and let them be medications that are moist in consistency but dry in potency, like the palm ointment when extra vitriol has been added to it. And among the most effective treatments is to take the palm ointment.
And dissolve it in rose oil and sprinkle aged wine of moderate consistency on it, then knead it with it and use it. And this same effect can be achieved when palm ointment is not available by using honey cooked until it thickens, or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, grind all together and sprinkle it on honey while it's on the fire.
Then spread it on a cloth and bind it over the cavity, or apply the honey that you cooked until thick and sprinkle the drugs on it and place the cloths over it and bind it.
And one may use for such cases long aristolochia, blue iris root, bitter vetch flour, and centaury - whether used individually or combined, ground and sifted and sprinkled on the honey I described, then use it. If the cavity's condition persists and isn't cured by this treatment, know that in its depths there is corruption.
And it has affected the bones or other hard nerves or ligaments, so treat it as a fistula as previously mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Nine
On treating paronychia, the crushed nail, cutting the extra finger, and splitting webbed fingers.
Paronychia is excessive flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb of hand or foot, and sometimes grows in other fingers. If its condition persists and its treatment is neglected, it swells with hot swelling.
Or becomes corrupt and suppurates until it eats away at the nail's root, and sometimes corrupts it entirely, and sometimes the corruption reaches the bone until it has a putrid smell and the fingertip becomes wide and its color becomes dusky. If you treat it with what we mentioned in the classification and your treatment isn't effective.
You should cut with iron all the excess growth from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterward, for cauterization in this case is very beneficial. If the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy.
And if the containing angle of the nail has pressed the flesh inward and began to pierce and harm it, you should place a thin probe under the angle of the nail that is piercing the flesh and lift it up, and carefully cut that flesh.
And place burning, corrosive medicines on what remains of the flesh until it all disappears, then treat it with ointments until it heals. If corruption has affected the bone, you should cut out that bone and remove it, for it will never heal as long as there is corrupted bone in it.
If you see the erosion and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it at one of the phalanges as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail suffers a blow or crush and severe pain occurs, you should first perform bloodletting on the patient.
Then split the nail with a sharp scalpel diagonally from top to bottom, being careful not to reach with the cut to the flesh under the nail, for you will cause severe pain to the patient and it will cause excess flesh to grow in the area, then treat the area with pain-relieving medicines.
As for the extra finger that develops in some people's hands, sometimes it is entirely fleshy, sometimes it has bones in parts of it, sometimes it has a nail, and some grow at the base joint of some fingers.
Or it grows on one of the finger's phalanges, and those that grow on the finger's phalanges do not move, while those that grow near the finger joint sometimes move. Those that are fleshy are easy to cut, as you cut it at its base with a wide scalpel.
As for those growing at the base of the joint, their treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting them. As for those growing on the finger at one of the phalanges, you should first cut its flesh in a circular manner down to the bone, then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion that occurs between fingers, this happens frequently and can be congenital or occur when a wound or burn heals and the like, so you should split that adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural state, then place between them wicks or cloths soaked in rose oil so they don't quickly adhere, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until the area heals properly.
And likewise, if adhesion occurs between some fingers and the palm, split that adhesion according to what is feasible and improves the shape of the organ.
Chapter Ninety.
On Cutting Varicose Veins.
Varicose veins are twisted, thick veins filled with black bile humors that occur in most body parts but most commonly in the legs, especially in the legs of farmers and porters. First, you should purge the body of black bile several times with strong purging.
Then perform bloodletting on the patient's basilic vein. As for treating it with iron, it is done in two ways: one is to cut and remove the black blood, and the other way is to extract the entire vein. As for cutting it, it is done in this manner: first bathe the leg well with hot water.
Until the thick, turbid blood dissolves, then bind the patient's leg from above his thigh to below his knee with a turban, then make wide cuts in the vein in one place or in two or three places.
Then massage the black blood with your hand from the bottom of the leg upward and from top to bottom until you remove the amount of blood you see as sufficient or what the patient's strength can bear, then bind it and order him to avoid foods that generate black bile, and repeat the purging and bloodletting.
When the vessels become filled and this harms the patient, its extraction is done in this manner: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair, then have them enter the bath and pour hot water on their leg until it reddens and the vessel becomes prominent, or have them exercise vigorously.
If no bath is available to warm the limb, then make a longitudinal incision in the skin opposite the vessel, either at its end near the knee or at its bottom near the ankle, then open the skin with hooks and separate the vessel from all sides until it becomes visible to the senses.
When it appears, you will see it as deep red, and when it is freed from the skin, you will see it like a tendon. Then insert a probe under it, and when it is raised and emerges from the skin, suspend it with a smooth blind hook, then make another incision near that incision about three fingers' width apart.
Then separate the skin from over the vessel until it appears, then lift it with the probe as you did before and suspend it with another hook as you did initially, then make another incision or multiple incisions if needed.
Then extract it and cut it at the end of the incision near the ankle, then pull it and extract it until it emerges from the second incision, then pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it from the third incision at the top of all incisions, and when it has all emerged, cut it.
If it does not respond to pulling and extraction, insert a needle with strong doubled thread and tie it and pull it, and insert the probe under it and twist your hand in all directions until it emerges, and be careful it doesn't break, for if it breaks, its extraction will become very difficult for you.
And it will cause harm to the patient. When you have extracted all of it, place wool soaked in wine and rose oil or olive oil on the wound sites and treat it until it heals. If the varicose vein is contracted with twists turning in different directions.
And if it is not straight as we said, you should make incisions on it at each of its twists and turning points, then suspend it with hooks until you extract all of it, and be careful when making your incisions not to cut or wound the vessel as it will make extraction difficult for you, so be as careful as you can.
Illustration of the scalpel used to incise the varicose vein:
Illustration of the blind hook:
It should not have a hook like other hooks and should not be sharp-tipped lest it wound the vessel, and it should be thick at the curve and smooth, because if it were thin it would cut the vessel due to its thinness, rather it should have extra thickness as we said.
Chapter Ninety-One
On Extracting the Medina Worm [Guinea worm]
This worm develops in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and Arab lands and in hot, harsh lands of little fertility, and sometimes it develops in other places of the body besides the legs, and its generation is from putrefaction that occurs under the skin.
As snakes, worms, tapeworms, and worms that develop between the skin and flesh occur inside bodies, and the sign of the beginning of this worm's occurrence is that severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the area blisters, then the worm begins to emerge from the site of that blister.
As if it were a plant root or animal, and when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams and tied, leaving the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever any of it emerges outward, wrap it around the lead and tie it, and if it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the rest, but do not cut it from its root before it all emerges because if you cut it, it will retract and enter the flesh, causing swelling and putrefaction in the area and a malignant ulcer.
Therefore, it should be managed and pulled little by little until it emerges completely and nothing of it remains in the body, and from some people this worm emerges with a length of five to ten spans.
And it has reached me that it extended in a man up to twenty spans, and if it breaks during your treatment, insert a probe into its opening and make a long incision along the body until all the material inside is emptied, and try to cause the area to suppurate with medications for several days.
Then treat it as you would treat tumors, and this vessel may have many branches, especially if it appears in the leg joint or in the leg itself, causing many openings with a branch emerging from each opening, so treat it as we mentioned in the classification and as previously described.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
On Incision for Worms Generated Under the Skin, Called the Cattle Disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries the cattle disease because it frequently affects cattle, and it is a single small worm that generates between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the entire body, ascending and descending.
It is perceptible to the senses as it crawls from one organ to another until it pierces wherever it pierces the skin and emerges, and its formation comes from the putrefaction of certain humors, as occurs with worms, snakes, and tapeworms in the belly.
The harm expected from it is that when it crawls in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it might open into it and emerge, destroying the eye, and this occurs frequently. If you want to treat it and remove it, this should be done when it is crawling and perceptible to the senses.
You should bind above and below it with a bandage tightly, then make an incision over it and remove it. If it sinks into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply cauterization with fire to the area until you burn it. The greatest harm to be expected is its destruction of the eye, as we said.
If you see it has reached the head near the eye, bind tightly below it on the forehead, then make an incision over it and remove it. The patient should maintain purification of their body with medications that purge putrid, harmful humors and avoid foods that generate putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On Incision for the Disease Known as "The Protruding."
This disease which is called in our country "The Protruding" is a pain that occurs in some organs then moves from organ to organ, and I have seen it as I will describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in some rural area, and she uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in the rope-like vessel of the arm. After I waited an hour, I saw that swelling crawling along the forearm like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
When it flowed from one place to another, the pain disappeared from that location and settled in the shoulder. Then I sat for an hour and it ran through the rest of the body until it reached the other arm. Then she told me that it circulates through her entire body as I had witnessed.
I was amazed at how quickly it moved from organ to organ, and I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this manner, though I had seen groups of people experiencing pain that moved from organ to organ, but I hadn't seen it with my eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I could only attribute this to the fact that the woman was from the rural area, with a dry body and exposed vessels, hence that moving wind was perceptible to the senses, and it would necessarily not appear in this way in people of luxury and moist bodies with hidden vessels.
If you want to treat it and the patient feels the pain, if it appears visible to you as we mentioned, quickly bind above and below it and make an incision over it until that confined wind comes out, and cauterize the area.
If you cannot see it with your eyes, treat it by purging the body and with what dispels and disperses winds, such as stinking pills and sagapenum pills and similar medications.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the locations in the body where they strike. As for their differences in types, some are large and some are small, some are hollow glass and some are solid glass, and some have three angles and four angles.
And some have barbs and some have splinters, and as for those that vary according to the organs they strike, they are of two types: either they strike principal hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder and similar organs.
When an arrow strikes one of these organs and the signs of death that I will describe to you appear, you should avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death will follow in most cases.
And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not disappeared deep into the organ, extract it and treat the wound. Among the signs of brain injury when an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane covering the brain, there occurs severe headache, dizziness, and vertigo.
And redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue and convulsions and mental confusion and vomiting of bile, and sometimes blood flows from the nostrils or ears, and sometimes speech is lost and voice disappears, and from the wound site flows a white fluid resembling porridge and something like meat water flows from it.
If these signs appear to you, refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow if it has not come out. As for the signs when an arrow strikes the heart, it will be near the left breast and feels as if it has been embedded in something solid, not in something hollow.
And sometimes the arrow has a movement resembling the pulse movement, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by cold extremities, cold sweat, and fainting, so know that death is inevitable. And the sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung is the emergence of foamy blood from the wound.
The vessels near the neck swell, the patient's color changes, they breathe heavily and seek to inhale cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the small ribs and the breathing will be great with severe pain.
And sighing, and all the shoulder organs move. If the arrow strikes the liver, this is followed by severe pain and blood resembling the liver in its redness flows from the wound. And if the arrow strikes the stomach, sometimes undigested food emerges from the wound.
And its matter is apparent. And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and becomes stuck in it, and something of feces emerges from the wound, or omentum, or a perforated intestine, there is no hope in treating it or extracting the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine comes out,
And something of it protrudes externally and the pain becomes severe for the patient, know that they are perishing. As for the remaining organs like the face, eye, throat, shoulder, upper arm, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they usually survive,
When the arrow does not hit an artery or nerve and the arrow is not poisoned. And I will tell you of some of what I have witnessed regarding these arrows for you to be guided in your treatment. That is that an arrow had struck a man in the inner corner of his eye at the root of the nose.
I extracted it for him from the other side under the earlobe and he recovered without any harm occurring to his eye. And I extracted another arrow from a Jew who had been struck in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had disappeared and I could only reach its small end which attaches to the wood.
And it was a large arrow from compound bows with square smooth iron without flanges, and the Jew recovered without any bad incident occurring in his eye. And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arabic.
This was the one who had two ears, so I made an incision between the jugular veins as it had sunken into his throat. I treated it carefully until I removed it, and the Christian was saved and recovered. And I removed another arrow from a man who had it in his belly, and we thought he would die from it.
When about thirty days or so passed and none of his conditions changed, I made an incision around the arrow, maneuvered carefully around it, and extracted it. He recovered without any adverse incidents. And I saw a man who had an arrow in his back, and the wound healed over it.
After seven years, the arrow emerged from the base of his thigh. And I saw a woman who had an arrow in her belly, and the wound healed over while the arrow remained, yet none of her conditions changed and she found no harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man who had an arrow in his face, and the wound healed over, and he remained without feeling much pain. And there are many such cases. And I removed an arrow from one of the Sultan's commanders which had struck him in the middle of his nose, having deviated slightly to the right side.
The entire arrow had disappeared, and I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I probed it with a thin probe but could not feel it. He was feeling a pricking pain under his ear on the right side.
I hoped that pricking was from the arrow's tip, so I dressed the area with a dressing that had drawing and ripening properties, hoping the area would swell and show signs of the arrow so I could make an incision, but nothing occurred in the area to indicate the arrow had reached that spot.
I continued with the dressing for many days, but nothing happened. During this time, the wound closed and the patient remained hopeless about its removal for a period until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. He informed me of this, so I applied a sharp, caustic medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it and felt the thin tip of the arrow that attaches to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine until the arrow's tip became visible to us, and this process took about four months.
Then when the wound widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved it but it would not come out. I continued to work carefully and tried various instruments until one day I grasped it with precise forceps, whose illustration will come at the end of the chapter.
Until I removed it, then I healed the wound.
The physicians had ruled that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound closed, and the patient recovered completely without anything hurting him at all. And I will tell you about the method of removing some arrows so you can use it as a standard and guide for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be comprehended through description alone, nor can any book encompass it. Rather, the skilled craftsman measures the many by the few, and what is present by what is absent, and develops new techniques and new instruments for strange occurrences.
When they occur in this craft. So I say that arrows are removed from the organs they are stuck in in two ways: either by pulling from the place they entered or from the opposite direction. Those that are removed from where they entered:
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy area so it can be pulled and removed, and if it doesn't come out at the time it struck, you should leave it for days until the flesh around it decays, making it easier to pull and remove. Likewise if it is stuck in bone.
And if it doesn't come out for you, leave it also for days and return to pulling and moving it each day until it comes out. If it doesn't come out after days, you should drill around the arrow in the bone itself from all sides with a fine drill until you make room for the arrow, then pull it and remove it.
If the arrow is stuck in the skull bone and has penetrated into one of the brain's ventricles, and some of those symptoms I mentioned to you appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it until his condition can be assessed after some days.
If the arrow has reached the peritoneum, death is imminent, but if the arrow is only lodged in the bone tissue and hasn't penetrated the peritoneum, and the patient survives for days without developing any of those symptoms.
Then attempt to pull and extract the arrow. If it is deeply lodged and doesn't respond to pulling, use the drills around the arrow as I described to you, then treat the area until it heals. However, if the arrow has disappeared into a part of the body.
And cannot be felt, probe it with the probe. If you feel it, pull it with some of the suitable instruments for pulling it. If you cannot manage this due to the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is no bone, nerve, or vessel there.
Then make an incision until the wound widens and you can reach the arrow to extract it. If it has two barbs that are holding it, free the flesh caught in them from all sides by whatever means possible, and if you cannot free the flesh, try to break or twist the barbs until they come free.
When attempting to extract the arrow from any location, use forceps with a twisting motion of your hand in all directions until you free it, and be extremely gentle lest the arrow break and make pulling and extraction difficult. If you cannot manage it immediately.
