Leave it for days until the flesh around it decays, then try again as it will be easier then. If you encounter bleeding, use the treatment we mentioned in its chapter, and take utmost care to avoid cutting any vessel, nerve, or tendon, and use ingenuity.
Use every possible means to free the arrow, and do this gently, deliberately, and carefully as I described to you. When pulling the arrow, you should position the patient in the same position they were in when the arrow struck, as this is most suitable.
If this isn't possible, use whatever positions you can. As for the arrow that exits from the opposite side, either some part of it has already protruded externally, or you find the arrow's tip by touch just under the skin and see it protruding.
Make an incision over it, and let the incision be just large enough to accommodate the forceps, then pull it as it will come out easily. If it's stuck in bone, rotate your hand in a circular motion until the arrow affects the bone and widens its path, then pull it.
Otherwise, leave it for days then try again until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still in it, push it with that, and if the shaft has fallen off and you want to push it, insert the hollow instrument so its cavity enters the arrow's tail, then push it with that.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters that hollow, as this will make the arrow's exit easier. If the arrow is poisoned, you should remove all the flesh that has been affected by the poison if possible, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow has fallen into the chest, abdomen, bladder, or side, and is close enough to feel with the probe and you can make an incision over it, then make the incision while being careful not to cut any vessel or nerve, and extract it.
Then stitch the wound if it needs stitching, then treat it until it heals. Description of the forceps used to pull arrows:
Their tips are like a bird's beak, made like a file, so when they grip the arrow or something else, they won't let go. They are made in various sizes - large, small, and medium - all according to the size of the arrow and the width or narrowness of the wound.
Description of the hollow pusher:
Description of the solid pusher:
This has a solid tip like a probe to easily enter the hollow arrow and push it.
Chapter Ninety-Five.
On the Phlebotomy of Blood Vessels.
The blood vessels that are traditionally bled in the body are thirty vessels, of which sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating vessels behind the ears known as the temporals, the two arteries that are visible in the temples, and the two vessels in the corners of the eyes known as the angular veins.
And the upright vessel in the middle of the forehead, and the vessel at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two vessels in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two vessels in the lower lip which are known as the jaharik, and the two vessels under the tongue.
As for the vessels that are bled in the arm and hand, they are five vessels: one is the cephalic vein which is on the lateral side and the common people call it the head vein; and the median cubital vein which is the middle vessel, composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the body vein.
And the basilic vein which is located on the medial side and is also called the axillary vein, and the common people call it the stomach vein; and the brachial vein which is located on the forearm and is attached to it, and it is the one that appears clearly above the thumb; and the salvatella vein.
And it is the vein between the little finger and ring finger having two branches, and in the leg and foot there are three vessels: one of them is below the knee pit on the lateral side, and the second is the saphenous vein located at the ankle on the medial side, and the sciatic vein located at the heel on the lateral side.
And in the other leg there are three similar vessels, and as for the two vessels behind the ears, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic catarrh, migraine, ringworm, and chronic malignant head ulcers, and the method of bleeding them is as I shall describe.
Which is that you should shave the patient's head and rub its back in the location of the two vessels with a rough cloth, rubbing it well, then the patient should constrict his neck with a turban until the two vessels appear, and their location is behind the ears in the depressed areas of the head, so you should search for them with your finger and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark there with ink.
Then take a knife-like lancet which is known as the nashl, then insert it under the vessel in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone, then raise your hand with the vessel and skin upward and cut the vessel with the skin in a decisive cut, and the length of the cut should be about two fingers pressed together.
Or thereabouts, and let out the amount of blood you want, then bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal, and their cutting and cauterization has been previously mentioned at the beginning of the book, and as for the two arteries in the temples, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and the flow of sharp humors descending to the eyes.
And the method of bleeding them is as I shall describe to you: the patient binds his neck with a turban until the vessels appear clearly to the senses and their pulse becomes distinct under your finger, then at that point mark with ink, then lift the skin above the vessel upward with your index finger.
And insert the nashl lancet from below and lift the vessel upward and cut it as you did with the other two vessels and let out blood according to your need, then release the patient's constriction and place your finger on the vessel for a while.
Then place cotton and a bandage on it and bind it firmly from above and leave it until it heals, and their mention, cutting, and extraction has been previously mentioned at the beginning of the book, and as for bleeding the forehead vein, its benefit after bleeding the cephalic vein is for chronic facial conditions such as ringworm, ulcers, and unsightly redness.
And the method of bleeding it is as I shall describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the vessel appears, then take the instrument called the axe and this is its form:
Place the protruding spike at the head of the axe on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or similar object, and let the blood flow as much as you desire, then loosen the patient's binding and bandage it until it heals.
It can be opened with a wide lancet, except that the lancet should not be sharp-pointed like other lancets, but rather slightly broad at the tip, and you should cut it at an angle because the bone is close, as the lancet might break if it is thin.
As for the two veins in the corners of the eyes, their benefit is for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, pannus, and facial diseases. As for how to cut them, the patient should bind their neck with a turban, then you cut them while standing at their head, and the cut should be slightly angled lengthwise with a small, somewhat broad lancet.
Since there is no flesh in the area, if the lancet is thin-tipped it might break. Then release the amount of blood you need and place cotton on them and bind them for one night, then loosen them. As for cutting the nose vein, it is beneficial for acute fever.
And severe headache and facial diseases like the red patches that appear on the nose, especially if chronic. The method of cutting it is to have the patient bind their neck, then hold their nose with your left hand and take a thin, long lancet.
Pierce it in the middle of the nose itself between the nose cartilages straight on, because the vein is not visible to the senses there, and the blood will emerge immediately. You should press your hand with the lancet slightly and release the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night and it will heal quickly.
As for the jugular veins, the benefit of cutting them is for shortness of breath, early leprosy, and black bile diseases that appear on the skin's surface like vitiligo, ringworm, malignant ulcers, and erosive conditions. The method of cutting them is to have the patient bind below them on their neck with a bandage, and the practitioner stands at the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, then you cut the vein lengthwise with a slightly wide cut, then remove a moderate amount of blood or according to what you see as necessary, then do the same with the other vein, then loosen the bandage.
Bind the two veins with moderate pressure so the patient doesn't choke, and leave it until tomorrow when the wound will heal. As for the jugular veins, the benefit of cutting them after cutting the cephalic vein is that it helps with mouth ulcers, gum disease, malignant ulcers, cracked lips, and malignant ulcers.
That occur in and around the nose. The method of cutting them is to seat the patient in front of you and bind their neck with a turban, then turn their lips and look at the two veins, one of which you'll see on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, and you can identify them by their blackness.
Also, there are thin black veins around them, so cut them with a clean cut. If it's unclear to you and you don't know which they are, aim to cut the largest and most visible ones, and do the same with the two veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, the benefit of cutting them after cutting the cephalic vein is for quinsy that occurs in the throat from uvula disease and mouth diseases. The method of cutting them is to have the patient sit before you facing the sun, and lift their tongue and look under the tongue for a vein on one side and a vein on the other side, and their color is blackish.
Cut them and be careful not to cut too deep because there are arteries underneath them, as bleeding might occur from those arteries. As for the three veins that are cut in the elbow, they are the ones traditionally cut in all people, and cutting them is done in two ways.
Either by piercing with a broad basil-shaped lancet or an olive-shaped one that's thin, or by slicing with a knife-like lancet which is the blade [and this is its shape], the shape of the broad basil-shaped lancet:
It should be wide as you see, suitable for opening thick, prominent, hollow, filled veins that contain thick, turbid blood, and this is the image of the olive lancet:
This lancet is less wide and has a finer tip, suitable for bloodletting from thin veins that contain thin, bilious blood, and this is the image of the needle lancet:
This needle lancet is suitable for incisions and comes in various types, wide and thin, according to the width and narrowness of the veins, and this can be used as a reference for others, and it is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of its bloodletting is that it draws blood from ailments below the throat and neck near the chest and abdomen. The phlebotomist must be cautious when bleeding it and be vigilant, for there is an artery beneath it, and if he errs and pushes the lancet too deep, he will cut that artery.
This will cause bleeding, therefore its bloodletting should not be done with a piercing lancet but rather with an incising needle lancet. If the basilic vein is not clearly visible, you should avoid it and turn to another, or seek one of its branches.
Or bleed the rope of the arm in its place, for it is clear, and incise it with the needle lancet as we said. If you want to bleed it specifically, before tying the arm, you should feel the area until you recognize the location of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then tie the arm and make an oblique incision in the vein with the needle lancet as we said, and try to make the strike away from the location of the artery. When you see swelling at the spot you marked with ink while tying the bandage, know that this swelling is the inflation of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood during bloodletting spurting like a child's urine, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood. Then quickly place your finger on it for a long while, then remove your finger; if the bleeding stops, as it often does, bind the arm.
Leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be vigilant and not move it for days until it heals. If the blood doesn't stop and overwhelms you, and you don't have medicine at hand, cut off the artery if it's visible to you, for its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it, take one half and bind it firmly over the vessel with bandages and compresses until the next day. If the bleeding stops, fine; if not, treat it with the aforementioned hemostatic powders. Stopping its bleeding is not difficult in most cases.
Due to the small size of the wound and the effectiveness of the bandage on the arm, know this. As for the median vein, the benefit of its bloodletting is that it draws blood from the top of the head and lower body because it is composed of a branch from the basilic and a branch from the cephalic, as we said.
The phlebotomist must be vigilant when bleeding it because there is a nerve beneath it, and if he pushes the lancet too deep and hits the nerve, numbness will occur that is difficult to heal and may never heal at all. This nerve is often visible to the senses.
If it is hidden in some people and is too thin to be distinguished, you should make your incision with the needle lancet and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, make a longitudinal incision in the vein. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its bloodletting is that it draws blood from the head.
It benefits eye diseases, and for this vein specifically, if you wish to bleed it, pierce it with the olive lancet or the wide basil lancet because it is the safest of all veins, as there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
However, during bloodletting, you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft spot. It doesn't harm it if it isn't hit on the first strike to repeat the bloodletting multiple times, although it may swell in some people if not bled on the first strike.
But that swelling does not harm him at all. As for the method of bloodletting, its symptoms, and what should be done to improve it - first of all, you should know that bloodletting is either used only for maintaining health.
Or it is used for diseases. That which is used for maintaining and preserving health and preventing the occurrence of diseases should be done in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, median cubital, and basilic veins, and the bloodletting should be done at the beginning of spring when signs of fullness appear.
And the bloodletting should be done on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed in the day. As for bloodletting used for diseases, it has no specific time, but rather whenever need and necessity call for it, whether night or day.
And at any hour and in any season. Children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years, nor should the elderly who have passed sixty years be bled. When someone intends to perform bloodletting for any reason.
One should clean their bowels before bloodletting with a gentle enema if there is much trapped waste, lest putrid waste be drawn into the vessels during bloodletting from the intestines, harming the vital organs. Neither the nauseated, nor the drunk, nor the intoxicated should be bled until that condition passes from them.
One should also avoid bloodletting following indigestion, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, fatigue, exercise, staying up late, fasting, and anything that weakens strength, whether physical or psychological. Then examine the thinning of humors before that if the blood is thick.
Through foods, drinks, and medicines if possible. Then enter the bath if nothing prevents it, or exercise somewhat so that the blood becomes thin, and perform the bloodletting in the early day as we said, and strive to free his mind that day from all harmful psychological conditions.
Such as worries, anger, fear, and from all physical conditions like excessive fatigue and exhaustion, sexual intercourse and the like. And prepare for his session the things that people have become accustomed to using, of various perfumes, aromatic plants, entertainment and such, each person according to what is possible for them.
Then the one performing bloodletting should sit on a cushion that is higher than the cushion on which the patient sits. Then he extends his arm and the practitioner rubs it with his hand two or three times, then tightens the bandage with the cord, twisting it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
Because when the tightening is not moderate, either excessive tightness prevents blood flow, or if it is too loose it also prevents blood flow. Then after tightening, the patient should rub both his hands together.
Until the vessels swell and become perceptible to the senses. Then the practitioner wipes the lancet with a little old oil specifically, then places his index finger of the left hand on the vein itself slightly below the place where he wants to make the cut, lest the vein dodge and the strike miss.
For among veins, some you find like a tendon that dodges during bloodletting, and some are filled with air, so when you place the lancet on them, they sink beneath the lancet and deceive the practitioner, and the lancet does not open the vein, or if it does open it, the opening is narrow.
Therefore, the practitioner should be steady and deliberate in all these matters. Then lower the lancet, and if it opens the vein on that attempt, good, otherwise repeat it another time slightly below or above that spot quickly if the area has not become swollen.
If it becomes swollen or the patient becomes anxious, leave it for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage as it might bring about hot swelling, and do not enter the bath. Then resume bloodletting if desired. If the lancet pierces and the opening is small and the blood flow is thin.
And if you fear that the amount of blood you want won't come out, reinsert the lancet into the same hole carefully and straight, and increase the opening slightly, and do this quickly before the area swells, for in many people the area swells even with a small opening.
If you see it has swollen, do not repeat [the procedure] at all, for it will not help you at all, and place some oil dregs on it, for it eases blood flow and is better in this situation than the oil itself and than other oils likewise.
Use oil dregs in all your blood-letting of vessels when blood flow is difficult, and theriac al-faruq and shakhzanaya also do this when either is placed on the area, for the blood thins and dissolves if it was thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the blood-letting site, which often happens especially to those who have never been bled except that one time or if the vessel opening was small, then hurry and place a sponge soaked in slightly warmed water and salt on the swelling and bind it for an hour, for it will dissolve.
This should be done after the blood has completely come out from the vessel itself or from another vessel. If some blackness or greenness remains in the area after days, this is not harmful, and if you wish, you can apply some dissolved aloe and myrrh to it.
Or some mint juice and the like. Often swelling and protrusion occur when letting blood from the basilic vein, so place your hand on it, and if you find it flattens when you press on it, that is a bad protrusion, so beware of putting anything we mentioned on it.
For an arterial hemorrhage might occur from it, but dress it with something astringent to harden the area, then treat it with other treatments until it heals. And you should draw blood from each person according to their strength and what appears of the dominant color of the blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it becomes red, and likewise if you see it is thick, let it flow until it thins, and likewise if it is sharp until its sharpness goes away. And for one who is full-blooded and strong and needs blood drawn at once.
The vessel opening should be widened and the lancet should be broad, and for one who is weak, the opposite of that, and you should draw it multiple times and the hole should be narrow. And the best method used in blood-letting is that it should be slanted and oblique, a cut not a puncture.
This type of blood-letting is safe from hemorrhage and nerve cutting, and it is more praiseworthy and safer than transverse and longitudinal blood-letting. And for one who habitually faints during blood-letting, they should be fed before the procedure some bread soaked in sour pomegranate water or oxymel.
If they are hot-tempered, draw their blood in three or four times, and if they are cold-tempered, they should take before blood-letting bread soaked in mebae syrup or in honey syrup flavored with spices or in fragrant wine.
If fainting occurs during blood-letting and its cause is excessive blood loss, they should be given meat water and light fragrant wine, and perfumed with galiya, and their chest should be loosened with it, and use the rest of what we mentioned in the classification in the chapter on fainting.
Which occurs from evacuation. As for one who wants to rest their arm and draw blood again, for one whose blood-letting was for much evacuation and whose strength is weak, the blood should be drawn little by little according to strength over consecutive days.
As for one who wants to rest their arm and draw blood again and whose body is strong, let them do that after seven or nine hours from their first blood-letting. And as for one who wants to draw blood from their body to the opposite direction to which it has inclined, they should rest on the second or third day.
As for one who has much blood in their body that has become hot and sharp and caused fever, blood should be drawn from them at once, and draw the large amount and widen the opening until fainting occurs, after having verified all the conditions for blood-letting.
Place your hand on their pulse while bleeding, lest death occur instead of fainting, for this often happens when the one performing bloodletting is ignorant and becomes careless, and when you want to release the arm and let blood flow again.
When the vessel's opening has closed and blood flow becomes difficult, you should not press forcefully or twist strongly, for this is very harmful. Either leave it until you can let blood again, or remove the clotted blood from the vessel's opening with the lancet's blade.
Or apply some salt dissolved in water, or apply some theriac al-faruq or shakhzanaya, and press gently until blood flows. If the vessel has become swollen, leave it.
Don't touch it until the swelling subsides. If there is a necessary need to let blood again, either let blood above that spot or let blood from the other arm or another vessel.
As for bleeding the rope of the arm (median cubital vein), it is bled instead of the basilic and cephalic veins when they cannot be found or are hidden, as it is composed of both. The procedure is to have the patient put their hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vessel becomes clearly visible, then tie above it with a bandage, applying moderate pressure.
Then bleed the vessel at a slight angle, neither across nor lengthwise, and make the opening wide. Make your incision slightly above the wrist joint. If blood flow is difficult, return the hand to the vessel with hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until you achieve your goal. In summer days, you may not need to return the hand to hot water. Letting blood flow into hot water is mostly done in winter time. Bleeding this vessel is safer than all other vessels.
Because there is neither an artery nor nerve beneath it. As for bleeding the basilic vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver conditions. The procedure is to tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand after putting it in hot water until the vessel swells.
And becomes very perceptible to touch, then bleed it at a slight angle. If you cut it completely, this won't harm it, but be careful not to push your hand too deep with the lancet as beneath it are the finger nerves and the area is devoid of flesh. Then return the hand to hot water.
Let the blood flow into it, for if you don't return it to hot water, the blood will clot at the vessel's opening and stop flowing. When you have drawn the needed amount of blood, put oil and salt on the vessel so it doesn't heal too quickly.
You should do the same with every narrow branch. As for the benefit of bleeding it from the left hand, it is beneficial for splenic conditions, and you do the same procedure as with the second one. As for bleeding the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower body such as uterine conditions.
Menstrual retention, kidney diseases, chronic thigh and leg ulcers, and similar diseases. The procedure is to put the foot in hot water and massage it until the vessels become prominent, then tie above the foot joint with the banda (bandage). The vessel's location is near the ankle, visible towards the big toe.
Many branches spread from it on the foot's surface. Bleed it at its widest branch or at the ankle where it converges, as this is better and safer. If you bleed it on the foot's surface, be careful of the nerves beneath it on the foot's surface.
Make your incision at an angle as if you intend to cut it completely, and let the lancet be elevated. If blood flow is difficult, return the foot to hot water and let the blood flow into it until finished. If the one performing bloodletting misses the vessel on the first attempt.
They should try bleeding slightly above, for the area is safe and no harm is feared if you are careful of the nerve as we said. Do the same with the saphenous vein of the other foot. As for the sciatic vein, its location is, as we said, at the heel on the lateral side.
The benefit of its bloodletting is for hip pain when it's caused by hot blood, and the method of bloodletting is to have the patient enter the bath quickly and bind their leg from the hip to four fingers above the ankle with a long, thin turban, for it will only appear with that.
When it appears, let blood from it in whatever way possible - either at an angle, which is better, or cut it completely, or make an incision, for its location is safe, though in most people it is very hidden. If you cannot find it,
And if it's not at all perceptible to the senses, then let blood from one of its branches, which appear on the top of the foot near the little and ring fingers, being careful of the nerves, and release the amount of blood you want.
Then loosen the binding and place cotton on the site of bloodletting and bind the area, for it will heal quickly.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On Cupping and How to Use It.
Cups can be made of horns, wood, copper, or glass, and cupping is done in two ways: one is cupping with scarification and blood removal, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping without scarification is done in two ways.
Either it can be done with fire or without fire, and the cups used with scarification and blood removal have fourteen locations on the body: one is the nape cups at the back of the head, and the kahil which is the middle of the neck.
The cups of the neck sides, which are both sides of the neck together, and the chin cups which are below the lower jaw of the mouth, and the shoulder cups, and the tailbone cups at the end of the tail, and the forearm cups which are in the middle of the arms, and the leg cups, and the heel tendon cups.
Cupping draws blood from the fine vessels dispersed in the flesh, and for this reason it does not decrease strength like bloodletting does, and cupping should not be used, whether with fire or without fire, in any diseases that come from fullness until the whole body is purged.
If there is need for cupping due to illness or habit, we use it at any time - at the beginning of the month, at its end, and in its middle, and in any season.
This is because among people, when blood increases in them until it needs to be removed by cupping, some find heaviness and headache in their head, and some find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck, and some find itching in their face and sides and darkness and irritation in their eyes, and some find itching in their cupping sites.
And some laugh excessively, and some find the taste of blood in their mouth and their gums swell and they spit blood, and some sleep excessively, and some see in their sleep blood and redness and the killed and wounds and similar things, so whenever we see any of this.
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed from the day, and as for the benefit of cupping the nape, it benefits heaviness in the head and water flowing to the eyes.
But this should only be done after purging the whole body, and this cupping can be a substitute for bloodletting from the cephalic vein, and one should be cautious about using it on someone with a cold brain or who has catarrh, for it will harm them greatly.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their head should not use it, and those who persist in using it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, you should instruct the cupper to lower his hand with the cup slightly downward for fear of causing forgetfulness, and as for cupping the kahil (middle of neck), it is a substitute for bloodletting from the median cubital vein and the basilic vein.
Therefore it benefits asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the breathing apparatus, coughing, and fullness, and the cupping of the kahil should be raised slightly because if it is placed lower, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
The cupping of the neck veins benefits pains occurring in the head, ophthalmia, migraines, throat pain, and tooth root pain, and it substitutes for basilic vein bloodletting. The cupper should be instructed not to make deep incisions to avoid cutting an artery which would cause bleeding.
Cupping under the chin benefits mouth ulcers, gum disease, and similar mouth ailments, and it can substitute for labial vein bloodletting. As for cupping between the shoulders, it benefits palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
Cupping the inner forearms benefits in ways similar to bloodletting from the three veins - the basilic, median cubital, and cephalic - because it draws blood from all those fine vessels in the flesh, and those fine vessels draw from other thicker vessels until the drawing reaches the three major vessels.
The cupper should be instructed not to go deep with the incision because the area lacks flesh and contains nerves and small arteries beneath. As for the single cup placed on the tailbone, it benefits hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large cup made of copper because the area requires strong suction, and glass cups might break, and to make multiple incisions. As for leg cupping, it notably reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and benefits chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, induces menstruation, benefits pustules and boils, and substitutes for saphenous and achilles vein bloodletting, though it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people. The benefit of achilles cupping is similar to that of leg cupping.
The method of applying cups is to first place the empty cup and apply moderate suction, not leaving the cups on for long, but rather placing and removing them quickly so the humors flow to the area evenly, and continue repeating this until you see the area has reddened and opened up.
When the blood's redness appears, then make incisions and gradually resume suction, then observe the body's condition. For people with soft flesh and open pores, one incision should suffice to avoid the area becoming ulcerated.
Instruct the cupper to widen the incision and deepen it slightly, and to moderate the suction with gentleness and slight movement. If the blood is thick, two incisions should be made - the first to open a path for the thin blood and its water content, and the second to complete the extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, make a third incision to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to extract a little blood, one incision suffices, but if we want to extract much blood, we make more incisions. If we determine the blood is thick...
The incision should be deep, and the moderate limit in incision is just the depth of the skin. Regarding which oils should be used when applying cups, which waters, and what the person being cupped should avoid - for those with thick, hard, dry skin and narrow pores...
The cupping areas should be oiled with opening, softening, and dissolving oils. If it's during summer, use oils like wallflower oil, violet oil, sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
If it's during winter, use oils like narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or iris oil and similar ones. If the excess is thick and cold, use marjoram oil, pennyroyal oil, moringa oil, dill oil, and similar ones.
If the cupper has wide pores and tender flesh, he should avoid oil, and these people should wash their cups after cupping with rosewater, cold water, nightshade water, gourd water, purslane water, and similar substances. As for those who have blood with excessive moisture,
They should wash their cups with vinegar or myrtle water and sumac water and similar substances. As for those who have thick waste products, they should wash their cups with aged wine or marjoram water or dill decoction or chamomile and similar substances. One should be cautious of cupping in the bath or immediately after bathing.
Rather, cupping should be done an hour or two after leaving the bath, and one should not sleep after cupping. What should be arranged for the person being cupped or bled before and after the procedure.
You must first observe if the person being cupped or bled is of choleric temperament with sharp and inflamed blood predominating, then they should take cooling substances like pomegranate, endive with vinegar, lettuce, oxymel, julep and similar things, and their food should be young chickens and lamb meat prepared as sikbaj and sour dishes and similar foods.
For those of cold temperament, they should drink honey syrup and myrobalan syrup or seeded oxymel, and take aromatic wine of medium age between new and old, and be instructed to eat little, with their diet consisting of young chickens, larks, sparrows, and squabs prepared as isfidhabaj, and on the day of cupping and bloodletting, drink should be more than food.
In some cases, for some people, they should be given theriac or musk medicine or shiltha before cupping and before bloodletting or after it to strengthen the vital organs and thin the blood, but those of hot temperament should not be given these.
As for cups without scarification, these are the cups placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, belly, navel, kidney area, and hip joint, because these organs cannot tolerate scarification, and the purpose is either to draw blood from one organ to another.
Like when we place cups on the breasts for nosebleed, or we use them to release cold wind that has become trapped in an organ, like when we place cups on the belly and navel, for it loosens the organ, warms it, and removes pain by dissolving that wind.
They may be placed on the kidneys when there is blockage or stones, for by their drawing power they might open the blockage or dislodge the stones from their place, and likewise they work when placed on the liver and spleen when wind is trapped in them. These cups may be used empty with suction only.
They may be used with fire, and they may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of sciatica, where the cup - which should be large - is filled with hot water alone or with water in which certain appropriate herbs have been boiled.
Then it is placed full on the spot and held, removed, and reapplied several times as needed. This is the form of the cup that is used with fire:
Its mouth should be two fingers wide as we have illustrated, and its depth half a span, and it should have in its side, about halfway up, a small hole the size of a needle's entry, made of Chinese copper or yellow copper with thick edges.
Smooth, even, and polished so as not to harm the organ when placed on it, and in its middle there should be a crossbar of copper or iron where the candle with fire is placed. These cups may be made larger than what we described and smaller according to the diseases.
And the age of the user, for the cups for children and thin people are not the same as those for men and thick-bodied people. As for how to place this cup with fire on the organ, it is that you light a well-made cotton wick or small tar candle with fire and place it on the middle of the cross-bar that is in the middle of the cup.
Let the fire rise upward toward the bottom of the cupping glass so that the patient's body does not burn, then place it on the limb with the finger on the hole we mentioned until when we hold the cupping glass as needed, remove the finger.
The vapor will exit through that hole and the cupping glass will release immediately, then light the wick as described and repeat it if needed, but as for the cupping glass used in treating water retention, it has neither a cross rod nor a hole.
Rather, it is used by filling it with water and placing it on the limb only, and this is its form:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to hold more water, the better.
Chapter Ninety-Seven.
On the Application of Leeches.
Leeches are mostly used on body parts where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either due to their small size like the lip and gum and similar areas, or because the part is bare of flesh like the finger and nose and similar parts.
The method of their use is to select leeches from fresh, clean waters free from contamination, then leave them for a day and night in fresh water until they become hungry and their bellies are empty.
Then first purge the body either by bloodletting or cupping, then rub the affected limb.
Until it reddens, then place them on it, and when they become full they will fall off, and if it's possible to suction the area with a cupping glass it is more beneficial in effect, otherwise wash the area with vinegar then with plenty of water and massage and squeeze it, and if bleeding continues after the leeches fall off and it's oozing, then wet a linen cloth in cold water.
And place it on top until the oozing stops, and if bleeding increases, sprinkle ground vitriol or oak galls or similar astringents on it until the bleeding stops, or place peeled fava bean halves on the area.
And leave them until the beans stick to the area and the bleeding will stop, and if leeches need to be reapplied, those same leeches should not be used if others are available, and if the leeches refuse to attach, then rub the area with fresh blood.
Or prick the area with a needle until some blood comes out then apply them, for when they sense some blood they will attach immediately, and if you want them to fall off, sprinkle some aloe, salt, or ash on them and they will fall off right away.
Book Three.
On Bone Setting.
This section is also among the most important needs in the medical profession, and it concerns setting fractures and dislocations that occur in bones.
Know, O my sons, that this field is claimed by ignorant physicians and common people who have never studied any book of the ancients about it nor read a single letter of it.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become non-existent in our country, and I have never met anyone skilled in it at all.
I only learned what I learned through my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them until I extracted this knowledge from them, then I committed to experimentation and practice throughout my life.
I have recorded for you in this section everything my knowledge encompasses.
And what my experience has confirmed, after I made it accessible to you and freed it from the complexity of lengthy exposition, summarized it to the utmost brevity, explained it with the utmost clarity, and illustrated for you many images of the tools used in it.
As it adds to the clarity, as I did in the two preceding sections, and there is no power except through God, the High, the Great.
Chapter One.
On general principles and comprehensive matters concerning bone fractures that must be presented, and before we begin discussing broken and dislocated limbs one by one, we should mention at the beginning of this section some general statements and points.
You must first understand these and grasp their truth, both you and whoever else is eager to learn this noble craft besides you.
I say that whenever someone suffers a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall, one should hurry first to perform bloodletting or purging or both.
Unless there is a preventing factor such as weakness, or if the person who has this condition is a child or a very elderly person, or if the weather is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then their diet should be restricted to cold vegetables and the meat of birds and young goats, and they should avoid drinks, heavy meats, overeating, and any food that fills the vessels with blood until the swelling is safe and no material is expected to flow to the area.
At that point, the patient should return to their original regimen that they were accustomed to. When the broken bone begins to heal, the patient should eat foods that provide substantial, thick, and strong nourishment with sticky consistency, such as crushed wheat dishes, rice, heads and feet [of animals], and cow stomach.
And eggs and fresh fish and thick drinks and such things, for with this regimen the fracture will heal faster and better. Know that broken bones in strong men and elderly people cannot possibly connect and heal to their original nature ever.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, while bones that are extremely soft like those of young children can connect and heal, but nature grows something like glue around the broken bone from all sides with thickness that sticks to it and binds it until parts adhere to each other.
And binds parts to each other until it becomes extremely strong and solid as it was before, such that nothing hinders its functions.
For this reason, the patient's diet must consist of foods that have strength, stickiness, and thickness as we said. Know that fractures have different types according to the difference in body parts, for a leg bone fracture is different from a skull fracture, and a chest bone fracture is different from a spine fracture.
And likewise all other body parts are different from each other, and we will discuss each type of fracture explained in its chapter, detailed separately from others. The type of bone fracture also differs in itself.
For it may be a clean break without splinters, or it may be a break along the length of the bone with splinters and separated or unseparated fragments, and the fracture may be with a wound and tear in the skin, or the fracture may be a slight crack.
Each type has its special treatment method for setting it, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. Among the ways to recognize a bone fracture are its crookedness, protrusion, being apparent to the senses, and its crackling when you press it with your hand.
If there is no apparent crookedness in the area, no crackling, you don't feel disturbance when touching the bone, and the patient doesn't feel much pain, then there is no fracture there, but it might be a sprain or a mild break or slight crack, so you should not move it with stretching and pressing at all.
Rather, apply the medicines that will be mentioned later as appropriate for the area, then bind it with gentle binding. Know that when a bone breaks and splits in two without developing splinters, but each part has moved away from its companion.
You should hurry to straighten and align it immediately before hot swelling occurs. If hot swelling occurs, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it in whatever gentle and skillful way you can.
Know that setting and aligning it is easier than with a bone that has developed splinters. Then it should be bound as will be mentioned. If the bone has splinters, the broken limb must be stretched from both sides, whether it's an arm or leg.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with both rope and hand. Place the limb on a level surface in its natural position until when the two parts of the broken bone are stretched, then work to return those fragments to their places in whatever skillful and gentle way you can.
Be extremely careful not to cause the patient pain or discomfort through your actions, and do your utmost to join the two bones together in the best possible position. At that time, you should touch and feel them with your hand, and if you notice anything abnormal, correct and adjust it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and strong pressing as many ignorant practitioners do, for through such actions they often cause hot swelling or permanent disability in the limb, as I have witnessed from their practice many times.
After alignment, perfection, and binding, ensure that limb remains still and at rest. Warn the patient not to move it while awake or asleep, when turning or shifting, during defecation, and in all movements to the extent possible, and to ensure that the position of the limb is one that prevents pain.
That is, whenever he feels pain or discomfort in the position of the limb, he should move it to a different position where he does not feel pain, while ensuring that this position remains even and straight.
So that no crookedness occurs in the limb when it heals. As for how to bind the broken limb, it is as I will describe to you. Know that broken limbs differ in their size and form - those that are small like the forearm, finger, and radius, and such.
For these, the bandage wrappings should be soft and light, while for thick ones like the thigh, back, and chest, the wrappings should be wide and firm, because a wide bandage adheres to and binds the large limb from all sides evenly without gaps.
Then after completing the alignment, begin by applying the appropriate medication.
Apply soft hemp fibers to the fracture site, then begin wrapping the bandage around the fracture site itself three or four times as appropriate for the limb, tightening your hand slightly with the bandage, then proceeding to the upper area from the fracture site.
With less tightness than at the fractured area, then gradually move away from the fracture site while gradually loosening the binding until you have covered a good portion of the healthy area. Then take another bandage and wrap it also around the fractured area.
Several turns, then proceed with the wrapping to the lower area from the fracture, and let your technique in tightening and loosening the wrap be as we mentioned for the first upper wrapping. Then place between the bandages soft hemp fibers or cloth to straighten any crookedness in the fracture.
If there is crookedness, otherwise don't put anything. Then wrap another bandage over it, then immediately arrange the firm splints over these wrappings if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply what will calm that inflammation and reduce the swelling, leave it for days, then bind the splints at that time. Let the splints be made from wide, carved halves of cane prepared skillfully, or let the splints be from the wood of sieves.
Which are made from pine, or palm fronds, or maple, or persimmon and similar materials that are available. Let the width of each splint be according to this form and this exact shape.
Except that the splint placed on the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints. As for the length of the splint, make it according to what suits the broken limb in terms of its size, whether large or small. The form of the splint:
Then bind over the splints with another bandage exactly as you did with your first binding, then tie it from above with firm threads as we mentioned regarding the binding, such that your binding is tighter at the fracture site.
And the further from the fracture, the less tight the binding should be.
The threads should be moderate in thickness and thinness, and should specifically be of moist linen because if the threads are thick like what I have witnessed from the practice of the ignorant who make their threads from twisted linen strips, it is a great error.
Because the binding with it falls outside moderation, and thin threads are also not suitable as you cannot achieve the desired tightness with them, and there should not be less than a finger's width between one splint and another.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after binding in the healthy areas, place soft hemp or fluffed wool under them so that nothing from it harms him, and if the fracture is accompanied by a wound and a tear occurs in the skin, we will discuss that separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be bound with splints from the first day, for if the limb is large, splints should not be placed on it except after five or seven days or more, depending on your confidence about the occurrence of hot swelling.
This is the description of the poultices that the ancients used to set bones, which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains. Description of a general abbreviated poultice that sets fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, as it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Take mill dust, which is the flour residue that clings to mill walls during the movement of the mill, knead it as is without sifting with egg white, and make the dough neither thick nor thin, then use it.
Description of another poultice that sets fractures, dislocations, and sprains: Take mung beans, ladanum, acacia, rasens, mugwort, and mastic, ten dirhams each, and myrrh and aloe, five dirhams each, and twenty dirhams of tamarisk, and twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay.
Grind all and mix with tamarisk water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then use this poultice, for it is well-composed, sets broken bones quickly, and is suitable for most people due to its moderation.
Description of another poultice also beneficial for fractures and sprains: Take mugwort, mung beans, and white marshmallow, ten dirhams each, myrrh and aloe, five dirhams each, acacia six dirhams, Armenian clay twenty dirhams, grind all finely, sift, knead with water or egg white, and use.
Description of a poultice for joints and bones displaced from their positions, which alleviates their pain and for bone cracks and fractures: Take washed wool, soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, and place it on the site. This poultice does not have setting power.
But it is excellent in calming hot swelling and especially in relieving pain. Description of another poultice that sets broken bones: Take deaf fig leaves and wild poppy leaves, grind them together and apply them fresh. Description of another abbreviated poultice used when the bone fracture is healing.
And you want to dissolve the remaining swelling: Take marshmallow root, chamomile, violet flowers, and bitter vetch flour, one part each, grind all and knead with wine if the limb is not hot, and if it is hot, knead it with coriander water or water and use it.
Description of another poultice stronger in dissolution than the first, used when hard swelling occurs during bone healing: Take marshmallow root, flaxseed, fenugreek, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, and chamomile, one part each.
Grind all and knead with willow water or fresh water or wine, all according to the limb's heat and the subsiding of its temperature. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book to place on the broken limb during its setting.
Except for cerotum made from wax and oil only, and he described it to be moderate between thick and thin. As for Galen, he saw fit to place on the broken limb during its setting things that have dryness with some heat, such as myrrh, aloe, frankincense, and similar to what we mentioned.
Description of a poultice beneficial for weakness and pain: Take mugwort, chickpeas, cut human hair or bird feathers, marshmallow, and salt in equal parts, grind, sift, knead and apply it. As for the duration that the bandage should remain.
Then he should remove it, meaning that you should observe if the patient doesn't experience pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its position, then don't remove it for many days. But if severe itching, disturbing pain, or swelling occurs in the area.
Then hasten to remove it immediately and don't delay that at all and remove the dressing from it. Then take a soft cloth or wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water and wash the area with it until the itching subsides and the pain calms, then let the limb rest for an hour.
Then apply to it fluffed wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil and bind it on for a night until you are safe from hot swelling and the limb's swelling subsides and its pain goes away, then return it to gentle binding and light dressing and don't bind it as tightly as your first binding.
Be gentle with it until it heals, and if you see that the swelling, redness, pain, inflammation and all symptoms have completely gone and you need the dressing and binding, then restore it exactly as you did the first time. If none of what we mentioned occurs in the limb.
Don't remove it except after three, four, five or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days, all of this according to what appears to you of the limb's condition as we said, until when the fracture has adhered and the flesh has nearly formed over it.
Then increase at that time the binding more than your first binding entirely and also increase the thickness of the patient's food as previously mentioned. If you see the fracture site has dried and thinned more than it should, know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Irrigate it with lukewarm water each time you remove it, and let that be every three days, and lighten the binding a little, for by this action nourishment flows to the limb and it heals quickly. As for what ignorant bone-setters do in breaking the limb again.
If it didn't heal properly the first time and healed crookedly, this is a mistake in their practice and a great risk, and if it were correct the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it, and I have not seen any trace of this from any of them at all.
The correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On Fractures Occurring in the Head.
The types of fractures occurring in the head are many, their forms are different, and their causes are diverse. Among fractures is what occurs from a sword strike, either cutting through the entire bone until reaching the membrane beneath the bone.
As an adze does in wood.
Therefore this type of fracture is called adze-like, or the sword may cut part of the bone and only scrape its surface without the cut penetrating to its end, and this type of fracture is called an absolute extraction, and the wound in these two fractures may be either large or small.
Among fractures is what occurs as crushing or bruising, its cause being a strike with a stone or falling on a stone or similar, and this fracture may either penetrate nearly reaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone.
The wound in these two fractures also may be either large or small. Among fractures is what occurs hidden in the bone as fine as a hair, and it is a slight crack.
Therefore this type of fracture is called capillary. Among them is a fracture occurring from a fall or strike of a stone or similar that pushes the surface of the bone inward and creates a depression in the area like what happens to a copper pot when struck so part of it goes inward.
This occurs most often in heads with soft bone like children's heads. All these types of fractures may have separated or unseparated fragments and we will come to mention the treatment of all that in its place. We recognize all these types of fractures by examining them.
And probing them with probes and removing corrupt flesh from them. As for the capillary type, it is recognized by exposing the bone and wiping it and smearing ink on it, for the fracture appears black. As for treating the fracture, first observe the patient's symptoms.
If you see among its symptoms what clearly indicates danger, such as bile vomiting, tension, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, protruding and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat them, for death is inevitable with these symptoms in most cases.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for their recovery, then proceed with treatment, such as when the wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded, and if this occurs during winter days.
You should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case, and if it is during summer days, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone becomes corrupted and those symptoms we described occur.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and is accompanied by fragmentation and crushing, you should cut the fragmented, crushed part as I will describe to you, which is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
Expose the bone in whatever way possible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what is tolerable for the patient. If you encounter bleeding or hot swelling when exposing the bone, treat it as necessary.
This means stuffing the area with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and clean the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding stops. Then proceed with hollowing out the bone and removing it, which can be done in one of two ways.
The first way is to cut the bone with a fine, narrow-bladed cutter, and this is its form:
Then use another cutter after this one that is slightly wider, and this is its form:
Then also use another cutter that is wider than the second one. You should have several different cutters, some wider than others and some shorter than others, with extremely sharp edges, made of Indian iron or good steel.
Be gentle when striking the cutter so as not to shake the head and harm it. If the bone is strong and hard, you should drill around it before using the cutters with what they call non-penetrating drills, and they are called non-penetrating drills.
Because they do not pass beyond the limit of the skull bone to what's behind it, as the drill has a circular edge below its sharp tip, similar to a collar or small circle that prevents it from penetrating and passing through the bone's thickness.
You should have many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have for each skull a drill whose sharp tip length corresponds to the thickness of that skull, and these are the forms of three types.
Of drills - large, medium, and small:
As for how to drill around the broken bone, place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated, then move the drill to another location and make the distance between each hole about the thickness of a probe or so.
Then cut between each pair of holes with the cutters, doing this with the utmost gentleness as we said, until you remove the bone either with your hand or with something else from the tools you have prepared for that.
Such as forceps and fine hooks, and you should be extremely careful not to let the drill or cutter touch any of the membrane. When you have hollowed out the bone and cleared it from the membrane if it was attached to it and removed it, you should strip.
And smooth the roughness remaining in the bone with another tool similar to the cutter, except it should be thinner and finer than the other cutters. If any small bones and splinters remain, remove them gently with the tools you have.
Then treat the wound afterward with wicks and ointments that will be mentioned. As for the other way of working, it is an absolutely easy way, far from risk, mentioned by Galen and greatly praised by him, and these are his words.
First you should begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and apparent, until when you have exposed that part, place under it the tip of this lenticular-shaped cutting tool, and this is its form:
The lenticular part of it should be smooth, not cutting anything, and its sharp part should be on its lengthwise sides as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the membrane while the sharp cutting edge faces the bone, then strike the cutter from one side.
With a small hammer until you cut through the entire bone gently as it curves, while being safe from [harming] the membrane - no fearful incident will occur at all, even if the practitioner is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were drowsy.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some places, scrape it away with the tip of the same lenticular cutting tool and separate it gently, for it will separate without harm or danger.
If the bone fracture has not penetrated to the membrane, and what is broken is the surface of the bone with roughness and fine splinters remaining, you should strip away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, of which several different sizes should be prepared so you can use whichever is most suitable and appropriate in each location according to what the procedure and the nature and shape of the broken bone guides you to.
You should first use the widest of these rasps in scraping the bone, then use one finer than it, and continue doing this in succession until you get to using the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the remaining small hair-like cracks and fine fractures, you should use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its repair - something that is not hidden from one who has even minimal experience in this craft and has studied what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, you should take a linen cloth sized to the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening, then take another cloth folded double or triple.
Soak it in wine and rose oil and place it over the first cloth, doing this as lightly as you can so as not to weigh down the membrane, then use a wide bandage over it and only tighten it enough to hold the wound closed.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are safe from hot swelling, then unwrap it and afterwards use medicines that have extra dryness like iris roots, bitter vetch flour, frankincense powder, birthwort and similar items - that is, make these medicines into a powder.
Sprinkle on the wound while they are dry, and in general, use in this a medicine whose nature is to cleanse without stinging, and strive your utmost while treating the wound to keep it clean without oil residue or dirt, and never let pus accumulate in it.
For if pus accumulates on the brain membrane it will corrupt and rot it, causing great affliction for the patient, and blackness may appear on the surface of the head membrane when the bone is removed from it, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If you observe blackness on its surface and the symptoms we mentioned appear in the patient, know that they will perish inevitably, but if the blackness occurred from medicine placed on it and it was in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness.
Then you should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well together, coat a cloth with them and place it on the membrane, then treat it with the types of treatment that are appropriate until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On setting the nose when it is broken.
Know that only the two upper parts of the nose break, either both or one of them, because they are bone, while the lower part is cartilage that does not break but rather suffers bruising, deviation, and flattening.
If one of its sides breaks, you should insert the little finger into the nostril and align that fracture from inside with your index finger and thumb from outside until you return the nose to its natural shape, and do this gently, taking care not to cause pain to the patient by your action.
If the break is in the upper nose and the finger cannot reach it, it should be aligned with the tip of a probe that has slight thickness, and if the break is on both sides, do the same, and hasten to set it on the first day of the break.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then insert into the nostril a wick made of linen cloth if the break is on one side, or insert two wicks if the break is on both sides.
Let the wicks be thick enough to fill the nostril, and some early bone-setters mentioned moistening the wicks with ghee and changing them daily, but I do not see this as proper; rather, the wicks should be moistened in egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the wicks until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens, and you may insert goose quill tubes in place of the wicks after wrapping them with soft cloth, which will hold the nose fracture more firmly and prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not essential - you may do it if you wish, or you may use the wicks, and if during your work hot swelling occurs, dress the nose with cerotum or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or some diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling occurs, you should dress it externally with semolina flour and frankincense powder kneaded with egg white, then place soft tow on it and do not bind the nose with anything at all, and if the nasal bones break into small pieces.
Or fragment, you should make an incision over them and remove them with the appropriate instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with healing and cicatrizing ointments suitable for that, and if a wound occurs inside the nose, you should treat it with wicks and use lead tubes.
Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On setting the lower jaw when it breaks.
If the lower jaw breaks and the break is not accompanied by a wound, examine it, and if the break is only external and has not broken into two and is concave inward, then recognizing it is easy.
If the break is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth, and likewise if the break is on the left side, insert the index finger of the right hand and gently raise the prominence of the break from inside to outside.
And your other hand from outside the bone controls its alignment, and if the jaw fracture has broken into two, traction should be applied from both sides in a straight line until alignment becomes possible, and if the teeth have become loose.
Or separated, tie those you hope will remain with gold, silver, or silk thread, then place cerotum on the broken jaw, then place a folded cloth on it, and place on the cloth a large, firm splint or a piece of shoe leather equal to the length of the jaw.
Then bind it from above as best you can bind it and as suits its compression until it does not come undone, and order the patient to rest and remain still, and make their food soft broths, and if you think something of the shape has changed in any way, hasten to unbind it on the third day.
Then fix what you dress it with mill dust mixed with egg white or with semolina flour after removing the cerotum from it and place soft tow on the dressing, and as long as that dressing stays attached to it and the bone's condition has not changed, leave it.
Don't remove it until the fracture heals and strengthens, for often this fracture strengthens in three weeks. If during that time hot swelling occurs, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to calm it until the swelling subsides.
As for if the fracture is with a wound, examine if a splinter or splinters have separated from the bone, then carefully extract those splinters with whatever tool is suitable. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a lancet according to your need.
Then when you have extracted those splinters and none remain, stitch the wound opening if it is wide, and if not, apply one of the ointments suitable for that.
And let the wound heal until it recovers.
Chapter Five.
On Setting the Collarbone When It Breaks.
The collarbone most often breaks at the front from the end of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks and splits in two without creating splinters, which is the easiest to set.
Or splinters occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to set, or the fracture is with a wound. The procedure when the fracture is without a wound is to have two assistants present, one to hold the upper arm next to the broken collarbone and the other to stretch the neck toward the other side.
Then straighten the fracture with your fingers until its shape becomes as it should be, without protrusion or depression. If you need more extension, you should place a ball of rags or wool under the patient's armpit.
Its size should be according to your need, and extend and lift the collarbone and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is straightened as needed. If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outward because it has gone deep, the patient should lie on their back.
Place a medium-sized pillow under their shoulder, and have the assistant press their shoulder downward until the collarbone bone that is deep rises upward, then fix the fracture and straighten it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the collarbone has broken off and become mobile, you should make an incision over it and carefully remove that splinter. If the splinter is trapped in the bone, work to cut it with one of the cutters you have prepared for that.
After placing under the collarbone the instrument that protects the membrane, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its form:
It resembles a spoon without a depression, and its width should be according to what you need based on the bone's size, large or small, and as for its length, also according to what is possible for the procedure, and it should have two ends as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound opening that you cut when removing the bone splinter is wide and you are not concerned about hot swelling, join the wound lips with stitching, and if the opening is small or you fear hot swelling, pack the wound with rags and pads according to the size of the incision.
If hot swelling occurs, moisten the rags in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for binding the bone when there is no wound or incision, apply the poultice made from mill dust with egg white.
Place soft tow on it, then place the ball under their armpit if needed, then take a very long turban whose width is a span or thereabouts, then place a folded pad over the tow and poultice, then take a splint from a thin board.
Its width should be three fingers and likewise in length, then wrap it in a rag, then wrap that rag with the splint in the place of the turban that falls on the fracture site, then bind the turban on the fracture as it circles.
Wind it around their neck and under their healthy armpit and under their injured armpit, and return it multiple times in every direction and however you see that the binding secures the fracture firmly, which should be clear to you.
The main concern is that the splint should not move from the broken bone.
Therefore, you should check on the patient every day, and whenever the bandage becomes loose and you see that the splint has moved, fix this and tighten the bandage, then have the patient sleep on his back and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
This is to raise his upper arm from his side, thus raising the collarbone fracture by elevating the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the bandage unless something occurs in the area like itching or swelling for twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see reason for that.
Return to tightening and leave it until the collarbone fracture heals and sets, and it typically strengthens and fortifies mostly in twenty-eight days.
And in some people it may be less than that.
Chapter Six.
On Setting Shoulder Fractures. The shoulder rarely breaks in its broad part but rather breaks at its edges, so whenever a location breaks or it breaks in its middle, this is known by touch, so according to the shape of the fracture, proceed.
Straighten it and return it to its natural shape by whatever means possible, then apply mill dust with egg white and soft oakum, and place above it a folded cloth pad, then place on it a wide splint made of thin board.
[The board should be] thin according to the size of the entire shoulder or slightly wider than it, and if there is any unevenness under the splint in any area of the shoulder, level that unevenness with soft oakum until the splint lies evenly, then bind it firmly from above with a long turban.
Bind it firmly and secure the splint so it doesn't move from its position, and check the bandage every day, whenever the bandage loosens tighten it and adjust the splint if it has moved from its position, and the patient should lie on their healthy side, and the shoulder heals in twenty.
Days or twenty-five days, and when this period is complete, loosen the bandage while you are safe as it is among the bones that one does not fear will shatter or break again, and if a splinter of bone protrudes and is pricking under the skin, make an incision over it and remove it.
And do what I mentioned in treating the collarbone fracture regarding calming hot swelling if anything like that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On Setting Chest Fractures.
The chest may break in its middle though this rarely occurs, while its edges break and fragment more frequently, and among its symptoms when its middle breaks is that it sinks downward and causes severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
Sometimes blood may be expelled and the depression in the broken bone becomes apparent and is not hidden to the senses, and to set it, have the patient lie on their back with a pillow between their shoulder blades, then press down on their shoulders and gather the ribs with hands from both sides and carefully straighten it in whatever way possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone returns to what it should be, then apply the dressing and oakum and place above it a splint of thin board made of willow or maple or similar light wood after wrapping it in cloth.
Then carefully bind it to the broken bone so it doesn't move, and pass the bandage around in circles to the back multiple times and tie it firmly, then check the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity calls for loosening it due to erosion occurring in the area or pain or swelling, then hasten to loosen it and remove the dressing and fix whatever has occurred through its proper treatment method then return the dressing if you see fit for that.
Place it and maintain the bandaging until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
On setting broken ribs: Know that breaks in the ribs typically occur in the thick areas near the back, while their front ends usually suffer bruising because they are cartilaginous. This condition is not difficult to diagnose through examination with the fingers.
To set them, straighten the break with the fingers in the most feasible way until the shape is properly aligned, then dress and bind the broken bone with a splint if needed. If the broken ribs are bent inward, the patient will experience severe pain.
And a pricking sensation like that which occurs to one with pleurisy, because the bone pricks the diaphragm, and the patient also experiences difficulty breathing, coughing, and significant blood discharge. This is difficult to treat, and the ancients have attempted many solutions for it.
Some say the patient's diet should include foods that generate bloating and wind so that the stomach expands and pushes the break outward. We dislike this approach lest it confirm the occurrence of inflammation if it hasn't already occurred, and if it has occurred, it will increase and reinforce it.
Others say to place a cupping glass over the area and apply strong suction, which seems more logical, except there is fear that the cup might draw waste matter to the weakened area. Some say the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
Place padding between the ribs until filled so the bandage will be even when wrapped around. Then treat the patient for pleurisy with food and medicine. If the patient is overwhelmed by severe discomfort that cannot be endured.
And if the bone is pricking the diaphragm harmfully and we fear for the patient, we should make an incision over the area and expose the broken rib, then place beneath it the instrument that protects the membrane as previously described, carefully cut the bone and remove it.
Then join the wound edges if it is large with stitches and treat it with ointments until it heals. If inflammation occurs during this process, hurry to moisten dressings with rose oil and place them on the area, and treat the patient internally with what calms inflammation.
Have them lie on whichever side allows them to sleep more comfortably until they heal.
Chapter Nine.
On Setting Vertebrae of the Back and Neck.
As for the neck bones, they rarely suffer breaks and more commonly experience bruising, as do the vertebrae of the back. If this happens to someone and you want to know if they will recover or not.
Observe if you see their hands have become flaccid, numb and lifeless, unable to move, extend or contract, and if when pinched or pricked with a needle they neither feel it nor experience pain, know that in most cases they will not recover and are doomed.
If they can move them and feel pinching and pricking, know that the bone marrow is safe and the patient will recover with treatment. If the same happens to the vertebrae of the back and you want to know if they will also recover or not.
Look at their legs, and if you see they have become flaccid and experienced what happened to the hands, then if they lie on their back wind and stool exit without control, and if they lie on their stomach urine exits without control.
And if they lie on their back and want to urinate, they cannot do so, know that they are doomed, so do not trouble yourself with treating them. If none of this occurs, the situation is less severe. The treatment for what has occurred is to attempt to calm the inflammation.
By placing rose oil alone or with roasted egg whites on the bruised vertebra, applying this three times during the day until the inflammation subsides, then apply one of the strengthening, drying dressings to the area, bind it with bandages, and order them to rest and remain still.
