Let them not sleep except on the side where they don't feel pain until they heal. If during the injury a splinter has occurred in the bone or something has separated from it, then you should cut open the skin and remove that bone.
Then join the wound lips if it is large by stitching, then treat with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx breaks, which is the tail end, you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the rectum.
And straighten the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible and accomplish the straightening, then apply the bandage and splint if needed, then tie it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, cut over it and remove it, and treat the wound with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On Setting Hip Fractures.
Hip bones rarely break, but if they do break, their fracture happens by splintering at their ends and splitting lengthwise and tilting inward, and the patient experiences pain in the area and pricking sensations and numbness in the leg on the side of the fracture.
To set it, pass your hand over it until you determine how the fracture is shaped. If the fracture is only at its ends, then straighten that fracture as much as you can manage until its shape resembles the natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their stomach until you can set that fracture. When you have straightened it, apply the bandage then place on it a splint of wood or leather and tie it firmly.
Do not fear the fracture moving or the splint shifting, and fill the hollow of the flanks with something until the binding takes evenly, and order the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side. If hot swelling occurs.
Then stop stretching and setting it until the hot swelling subsides and apply what will calm it as previously mentioned, then return to setting and binding it as necessary. If splinters occur in the bone or something breaks off from its ends, it should not be removed.
Nor should it be touched, but rather straightened from the outside as we said and leave its binding until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On Setting Humeral Fractures.
The humerus is what lies between the elbow and the head of the shoulder. If it breaks, its setting is done in one of two ways: first, take a curved, smooth stick of medium thickness like this shape:
And tie two bindings at its ends, then hang it from an elevated place and have the patient sit on a chair, then place their broken arm over the stick until their armpit is pressed against the middle of the stick's curve, then hang something heavy from above.
Or have an assistant pull it downward while the physician straightens the fracture with both hands until the fracture is properly reduced. The other method is to have the patient lie on their back and hang their hand from their neck with a binding.
Then order two assistants, one to hold above the fracture with their hands and the other to hold below it, and each one pulls toward their side. If you want the pulling to be stronger, tie a binding below the fracture and another above it, and have each assistant pull them toward their respective sides.
If the fracture is close to the shoulder tip, you should place [the middle of] the binding under the armpit and the other below the fracture toward the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, you should place the binding on that spot and on the elbow itself.
Then straighten the fracture gently without force until when the fracture is properly aligned and well-joined, then bind it if there is no hot swelling. If hot swelling occurs, leave its binding until the seventh day and place on it wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until when the swelling subsides, then bind it. The method of binding it is to apply the dressing on the fracture, then apply a bandage of new cloth over the dressing, then bring the forearm to the humerus itself and place their hand open on their shoulder.
Bandages and bindings should be applied to the upper arm and forearm so that the forearm acts as a splint if nothing prevents you from doing so and if nothing has changed in the broken bone. If you fear that something might change, then use splints.
You should place on the break itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and there should be a finger's width between each splint, and the length of the splints should be according to the break with an addition of three fingers from each side.
Then bind the splints with the binding that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
Your binding should be tightest at the site of the break.
And the further from the break, the less tight the binding should be. If you see fit to place the splints and binding as we said immediately when setting the limb, then do so.
If you fear hot swelling, leave off the binding and splints until the seventh day as we said, then check the bandage every three days lest there occur in the area itching or swelling or the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, so fix all of that as we mentioned.
If you are confident that none of this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage except after many days, and the patient should lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and a pillow filled with wool should be placed under the upper arm at a moderate height.
Check at all times, day or night, lest the shape of the broken limb become displaced or the bandage become loose, so fix all of that with your effort, and make the patient's nourishment according to the level we previously prescribed, that the nourishment should be light at first until the bone begins to harden.
Then you should thicken his nourishment, for it is the custom of the upper arm and leg to harden in forty days, at which time you should loosen it and use the bath and treat with ointments that are suitable for that. If the break is severe and crushed,
Then do not loosen the bandage until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve
On Setting Broken Forearms
The forearm is composed of two bones called the two zands (radius and ulna): one small, which is next to the thumb, and the other large, which is positioned below the small one from below. Sometimes the larger zand breaks alone or the smaller one alone, and sometimes they break together.
When the small upper zand breaks, its setting is easy and its healing is faster, and when the lower zand breaks, its break is worse and its healing is difficult, and worst of all is when both bones break together. If the bone that is broken is the small upper zand,
The physician should, when setting it, make its extension slight and gentle until he aligns it, and if it is the large zand that is broken, he should make its extension stronger, and if both zands are broken, he should make the extension very strong indeed.
The hand should be positioned when setting and extending it stretched out on a pillow, with the thumb of the hand higher than all the fingers and the little finger lower than the rest of the fingers, and the patient sitting cross-legged.
The pillow should be at his level in height so the patient does not endure hardship.
Then an assistant should extend the forearm from below either with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant should extend from above likewise, then the physician should align the bone until he returns it to the best shape possible. If there are splinters in the broken bone, try
To return each splinter to its place with your best effort, and if a separate splinter appears that is pricking the skin and you have no hope of setting it, then cut into it and extract it in the manner we described previously. If the break is with a wound, I have devoted a chapter to it.
So take the treatment of that from there. If at the beginning of your setting a hot swelling occurs, then smear a cloth with cerotum made with rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between thick and thin, and bind the cloths on it with a gentle binding until the swelling subsides.
Remove the wax dressing and apply the poultice prepared from mill dust mixed with egg white, then apply the splints. The splint placed directly on the fracture should be slightly wider and stronger, and know that the number of splints for the forearm is typically six.
Whether the fracture is in one or both forearm bones, make your binding stronger and tighter at the fracture site, and as you wrap above or below, make the binding slightly looser as mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
The bandages wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist cloths, not too rigid, and the binding thread should specifically be made of cotton, medium between thin and thick as we described. Check the limb and bandage after several days.
If something occurs that needs correction, such as itching in the limb, the limb should be washed with warm water until the itching subsides, and leave the limb unbound for a night to rest, then resume binding.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted or similar issues, correct all of this to the best of your ability. Also observe if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive binding, in which case you should loosen it slightly and leave it for days until nourishment flows to it, then bind it again.
If none of what we mentioned happens to the patient, it should not be unwrapped except after twenty days or so. Then hang the patient's hand from their neck, keeping the forearm moderate, and they should be careful to avoid disturbing movements and sleep on their back.
Know that this forearm fracture heals in thirty days or thirty-two days, and sometimes heals in twenty-eight days, all according to the conditions of temperaments and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On Setting Fractures of the Palm and Fingers.
The metacarpals and finger phalanges rarely experience fracture, but rather commonly experience crushing. When the palm experiences fracture or crushing.
The patient should sit cross-legged with a level chair in front of them.
Then place their hand extended upon it, then an assistant extends the broken bones and the physician aligns them until when they are well-aligned, then the dressing and oakum should be applied if there is no hot swelling, then apply a splint from above according to the size of the area.
Having wrapped it in a soft cloth, if the fracture is downward toward the palm's interior, make a ball-like shape from cloth and order the patient to grasp it with their broken palm, then bind it with a long cloth.
The splint should be made of leather with some flexibility so the skin adheres with the bending of the entire palm, and bind it as necessary. If the fracture is outward, you should place the splint from above and another splint below in the palm so the hand remains open and upright.
Then apply the binding as the hand turns and interlace between the fingers with the bandage. If the fracture occurs in one of the finger phalanges, if it is the thumb, align it as necessary then bind it with the palm, and if you wish, make for it a small upright splint to keep the fracture.
And not moving. If the fracture is in the other fingers like the middle finger, index finger, little finger, or ring finger, align it and bind it with the adjacent healthy finger or bind them all in sequence which is better, or place upon them a small upright splint.
As we said regarding the thumb, and check during your setting and afterward for hot swelling, and address it as necessary whenever something of that occurs, as has been repeatedly described.
Chapter Fourteen.
On Setting a Broken Thigh.
The thigh bone frequently breaks and is perceptible to the senses because it shifts forward and backward, and setting it is done by tying one bandage above the break and another bandage below the break, with the patient lying face down.
Then an assistant pulls each bandage in its direction evenly - this is if the break is in the middle of the bone. But if it is close to the base of the thigh, you should tie a soft bandage, either of wool or similar material.
Near the base of the thigh towards the pubic area so the pulling occurs upward, and the other bandage below the break, and likewise if the break is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee so the pulling occurs downward. Then the physician aligns the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural form.
The bone should join together well, and at that point the dressing and binding should be applied if no hot swelling occurs in the limb. If swelling occurs, leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to treating it.
As for binding it, you should wrap a firm, wide turban around the break two or three times with some excess remaining. Then bend the leg until the heel is near the buttocks, and insert a long thread between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, making the ends of the thread come out above from both sides.
Then wrap the excess of the turban around the leg and thigh, then place splints on the thigh at the break site itself, and place one splint on the leg bone, then fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft rags so the binding is even.
Then begin with strong binding from the middle at the break site with three or four turns, and as you move away from the break site with the bandage, make your binding progressively lighter, softer, and looser.
Then take the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg and tie them to the nearby upper splints, then pass the thread ends downward until you reach the heel tendon, and with them also tie the ends of the splints from the other side.
So that the bandage doesn't move from its place, then leave the binding as long as no erosion, swelling, or inflammation occurs in the limb and such. If any of this occurs, hasten to loosen it and fix whatever has occurred according to what we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a bone splinter that pricks, you should align it if possible; if not, then cut down to it and extract it, and treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals. This thigh fracture can also be bound without attaching the leg to it with splints.
As we mentioned for the upper arm and forearm, except that setting it this way doesn't cause the patient to limp, while if you set it alone without joining it to the leg, the patient will inevitably limp forever.
Know that the thigh strengthens in fifty days, give or take a little, all depending on the variation in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On Breaking of the Kneecap.
The kneecap rarely breaks but is often bruised, and if it does break, it's either a split or fragmentation of its parts, and this can occur with or without a wound.
You can determine all of this by examination, and setting it is done by aligning the separated parts with the fingers until they come together and join as much as possible with gentleness and precision, then apply the dressing and place a rounded splint on it if needed.
Then bind it from above with appropriate binding, then monitor all the conditions we described in other fractures, such as hot swelling and such, by treating each symptom with what suits it until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On Setting Leg Fractures.
The leg has two bones, one thick which is called the leg bone, and the other thin which is called the fibula, and they are subject to the same types of fractures that occur in the two bones of the forearm.
Therefore, setting it is like setting the forearm exactly and the procedure is the same; if both bones break, the leg turns in all directions, and if the thinner bone breaks, the leg turns forward, and if the thicker bone breaks, especially from below, this will not be hidden from you.
Apply extension and alignment and bind the splints accordingly, except that if the leg fracture is severe with many fragments, the extension should be lesser and gentler, and you should be as careful as possible in setting it.
For the leg, there is an additional procedure beyond that of the forearm, which is that after you have aligned the splints and completed all your work, take two wooden strips made of pine that are used in flattening rooms which are placed between cracks in boards, or they can be from palm fronds or similar material.
Choose ones that are slightly thick and not from the thin ones, and let their length be the length of the leg from the knee downward, then wrap each one with cloth twice along its length and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side.
Let them extend from the knee to below the foot, then tie the strips in three places at both ends and the middle, for with this restraint the leg is prevented from leaning right and left and is properly aligned, and a wooden trough can be used along the length of the leg.
It is placed in it to prevent movement, and this should especially be done in fractures when there is also a wound, then examine the leg every day and pay careful attention to swelling or inflammation or anything else of that nature.
When any of these occurs, treat it as necessary until it heals, and the leg bone heals in thirty days or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen.
On Fractures of the Foot Bones and Toes.
As for the ankle, it never experiences fracture, but the foot bones can experience fracture, and the toes also rarely experience fracture but rather usually experience crushing.
If the foot bones experience fracture and you see those bones protruding over each other, have the patient place their foot upright on the ground as if walking, then stand and place your foot on what has risen of those bones and press down and align them.
Until they return to their positions, then apply the dressing and lint from above and place under the sole of the foot a small board that has two flat ends, then bind the foot firmly after wrapping it with cloths and whatever else you need.
When three or four days have passed, loosen the binding and you will find the bones aligned whether they were broken or dislocated, so the procedure is as you see, and if some toes break, set them and align them according to what I described to you for setting fingers.
Then make for the broken toe a splint along the length of the toe and let it be slightly wider than it, then place under the foot this board which I described to you and bind it firmly, and if what is broken of the bones is two or three or more.
Make for each toe a splint of its size wrapped in soft cloth and bind the board to the bottom of the foot, and let each end of the two ends of the board extend beyond the sole of the foot to secure it properly.
You should not forget to monitor everything we mentioned to you regarding all fractures for the symptoms we discussed and treat each symptom as necessary.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of a woman's vulva, pubic bone, and a man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then bend her gradually backward, and have her be held from behind. Then the midwife should stuff her vulva with cotton until it fills it and becomes like a ball in her vulva.
Then shake the woman and raise her back gradually, for that cotton will come out until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the vulva and the bone fracture returns [to place]. Then place a compress on her back, and when she needs to urinate, remove the cotton gently so she can urinate.
Return it as it was first inserted, then return to her first compress. Do this for seven days or so and it will heal. If you wish, you can take a sheep's bladder, tie a reed tube to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva, for the fracture will return [to place]. Then stuff it with cotton and maintain this for days as we described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone is fractured in a man or woman, use in its setting...
And the correct approach to these unusual fractures that rarely occur should not be unclear to you, especially for one who has some experience and understands this book of mine well, because the fracture itself will guide you in most cases...
As for a man's penis when it is fractured, take a goose's windpipe and insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of cloth over it and bind it and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen
On Setting Bone Fractures When Accompanied by a Wound
For one who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if it is a large bone like the thigh or upper arm or similar, you should hasten to bleed them immediately if the conditions for bloodletting that we previously mentioned are met.
If the wound is bleeding, you should hasten to stop it by sprinkling ground vitriol on it if nothing else is available, then proceed with setting the fracture on that same day and do not delay it.
If no hot swelling occurs. But if hot swelling occurs, leave its setting until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides, and do not approach it on the third and fourth day at all, for it will cause bad symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin and exposed, you should attempt to return it and align it with your hands gently and with slight traction. If you cannot return and align it with your hands, return it with this instrument, which is made of iron and is about seven or eight fingers long.
And its width according to the size of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of these, according to what is needed for treatment in each type of fracture, and they should be rounded with some thickness so they don't bend when pressed during the procedure.
And it should be sharp at the tip with a curve at its end, and its upper part should be thicker while from its middle to bottom much thinner, and this is its form:
It is called in Greek "pirim" meaning small lever. You should place its sharp, curved tip on the protruding bone end and push it with it at once until when the bone returns and aligns somewhat, then attempt to align the fracture ends with each other.
If its broken end is thin and the instrument cannot grip it well, you should cut the end of that bone until the instrument can grip it. If you cannot return the bone at all with what we described, cut it with whatever suitable cutting instruments we mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws however possible for you, then strip away what remains of roughness and thin scales in the bone. If after you return the bone the patient feels severe, harmful pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can return it to its natural position, do so for you will benefit the patient greatly. When you complete setting the bone, dip a cloth in astringent black wine, especially if you are in summer, and do not put on the wound any cerates or anything containing oil.
So that no rot or corruption occurs in it, then use splints when you finish setting the bone and leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors according to the size of the wound.
Be extremely cautious not to bind the wound with the fracture, as ignorant physicians have often done this and caused their patients either death, gangrene, or inflammation. Your binding should be soft and loose, different from the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large and you fear some of the dangerous symptoms we described, and there is disturbing pain in the area, you should not put splints on it but make bandages from strong cloth in place of splints and bind it with these.
After a day or two, when you see pus beginning to form in the wound, remove the cloth that was placed on it with wine, then use wicks and ointments that we customarily use to treat wounds, like the quadruple ointment and similar ones, and you should loosen the bandage.
Check the wound twice daily, morning and evening, until it heals and recovers, and you should position the limb in a way that allows pus to drain downward easily. If many days pass and the wound doesn't heal and the pus doesn't stop, know that there are small bone fragments there.
You should probe the wound with a probe, and whatever fragments are loose, extract and remove them. Those that are not loose and are pricking the limb causing pain, attempt to cut and extract them by whatever means possible.
If the wound develops inflammation, gangrene, or another type of corruption and rot, you should treat each symptom with the corresponding treatment that was previously described in its chapter.
One thing you must note and keep in mind is that if a large bone breaks and protrudes from the limb, like the thighbone or upper arm bone or similar large members, do not attempt to pull or extract it.
Often death results from this, rather leave it until it rots, as it might fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
At that point, treat the wound if you see a possibility for treatment, otherwise leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the knot that occurs following some fractures.
This knot often occurs following the healing of fractures, especially near joints, making the limb's appearance ugly and sometimes preventing its natural function.
Look - if the knot is fresh, use astringent medicines like aloe, frankincense, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia and similar substances, taking some or all of these and kneading them with astringent wine or egg white or vinegar.
Apply this to the knot with hemp fiber and bind it well, leaving the binding undisturbed for many days, then unbind it and repeat with another until the knot disappears, God willing, or bind a sheet of lead firmly over it.
For lead has a special property that removes all protrusions in the limbs. If the knot has hardened and strengthened, and necessity compels its removal, cut into it from above and cut away the protruding excess or scrape it with some scrapers until it's gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On treating a fracture when it has healed but the limb remains thereafter thin, contrary to its original nature.
When bone fractures heal and the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this happens for many reasons.
Among them are either frequent loosening and improper rebinding of bandages, or excessive tightness of bandages preventing nourishment from flowing to the limb, or excessive fomentation, or inappropriate movements at the wrong time, or lack of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich their body until blood increases in it, and use the bath, bring them happiness and joy and such things, then apply pitch to the limb so the pitch draws much nourishment to it, or continue fomentation with lukewarm water.
Until the nourishment flows in it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On treating broken bones when they have healed crooked and prevented their proper function.
When a limb that has healed after recovery becomes crooked, or the broken bone protrudes, or becomes knotted, and the appearance of the limb becomes ugly because of this.
However, if the limb is not prevented from its natural function, you should not accept the claim of one who suggests breaking the limb anew, though many ignorant physicians and bone-setters in our country used to do this.
This practice is very reprehensible and leads to great risk, the least of which is damage, but if the crookedness and knotting is fresh, it should be treated with water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as marshmallow leaves and its root, melilot, and the like.
It should be bandaged with softening dressings like well-made diachylon, or take marshmallow root mucilage and mix it with chicken fat and sesame oil and bandage with it, or take fatty figs and pound them with pigeon droppings and similar medicines that are called scar-breaking.
The knotting may dissolve with continuous gentle massage done by hands, and the limb should be moved in all directions at all times. If the crookedness is old and has become severe and hardened, and necessity calls for treating it with iron.
Then you should cut above it and release the bone's connection and cut away the excess knotting or bone with fine cutters, and do this with utmost gentleness and care, then treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On the Discussion of Dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its position, which hinders movement, disfigures the shape of the limb, and causes the patient severe pains and aches. When someone suffers a dislocation, it should be reduced immediately.
Do not delay it at all, for if delayed, the area will swell making it difficult to reduce the dislocation, therefore it should neither be delayed nor moved nor stretched while swollen because it often causes spasms and harmful pains to the patient.
But if this occurs, one should hasten to bleed the patient, then leave it until the swelling subsides a little, then bathe the limb with hot water and oil, then reduce it gently and treat each limb as will be mentioned in its place.
I have arranged the chapters on dislocation also according to what preceded in fractures from the top of the body to its bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On treating dislocation of the lower jaw.
The jaws rarely dislocate except in rare cases, and their dislocation occurs in two ways: either they shift slightly from their positions and become somewhat loose, or they dislocate completely and fully until they loosen.
Towards the chest until the patient's saliva flows and they cannot contain it nor can they close their jaw, and their tongue stumbles in speech. As for if the dislocation is slight, it usually returns on its own with minimal intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, you should hasten to reduce it quickly and not delay it at all. An assistant should hold the patient's head and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side.
Or insert both thumbs if the dislocation is on both sides, with the rest of the fingers outside to align it, and order the patient to relax their jaw and allow it to move in any direction while the physician aligns the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its position.
If it is difficult to reduce, especially if both jaws are involved, use hot water and oil fomentation until they are easy to reduce, and do not delay their reduction at all as we said. When they return and align and the patient's mouth closes and they do not loosen, then place cloth pads with a ceratum made of wax and rose oil on them.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage, and have the patient sleep on his back with his head stabilized between two pillows so he doesn't move it right or left, and he shouldn't attempt to chew anything but rather take his nourishment as soft gruel.
Until when the pain subsides and the jaw sets, then he may eat whatever he wishes, but he should do so gently and not force his mouth open when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw sets and heals.
If it becomes difficult to reduce the jaws when they are dislocated at some point and do not return to their positions, this often results in fevers and persistent headache, and sometimes the patient's bowels become loose, and sometimes he vomits pure bile, so when you see this, know that it is fatal.
And many who experience this die within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On Reducing Dislocation of the Clavicle and Shoulder Tip.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side due to its connection to the chest, but it may dislocate outward and this is perceptible to the touch, and its reduction requires the patient to lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the area firmly with your palm and it will return, then apply the dressing and pads and bind them, and as for its end which joins the shoulder and connects to it, it rarely dislocates, but if it should dislocate one day, it should be reduced and aligned as we mentioned and as is possible for you.
Then apply the dressing, pads, and binding, and order the patient to maintain rest and stillness until he recovers, and with this very same treatment you reduce the shoulder tip when it also moves from its position.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On Reducing Shoulder Dislocation.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one is that it dislocates downward toward the armpit, the second is that it dislocates toward the chest, and sometimes it dislocates upward above the shoulder, though this is rare.
It does not dislocate backward due to the scapula, nor forward due to the nerves, and most commonly it dislocates and comes out downward toward the armpit, especially in those with little flesh because in them it comes out quickly and goes back in quickly.
As for those with much flesh, it is the opposite, meaning it comes out with difficulty and goes back in with difficulty, and sometimes some people suffer a blow or fall causing the shoulder to swell with hot swelling, so it is thought to be dislocated, thus you should examine this until you ascertain its truth.
Then proceed with its treatment, and the dislocation is recognized when it is downward toward the armpit by comparing between the dislocated shoulder and the healthy shoulder, for you will find an obvious difference between them and find a depression in the shoulder head, and under the armpit upon palpation the shoulder head feels like an egg.
The patient cannot raise his hand to his ear nor move it in all directions, and likewise if it dislocates toward the chest or upward, you will find this obvious to touch and not hidden.
And this dislocation may be easy to reduce when it is recent or when the patient is a youth, and its reduction requires an assistant to raise his hand upward, then you place your thumbs under his armpit and forcefully lift the joint upward to its position while the assistant raises his hand and extends it upward.
Then he lowers it downward and it will return quickly, but if it does not return by what we mentioned and the dislocation has been present for many days, the patient should bathe in hot water and use an emollient and softening fomentation such as cooking marshmallow root, fenugreek, and melilot in water and applying it.
Then have the patient lie on his back and place under his armpit a wool ball of moderate firmness between soft and hard, then the physician places his heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand and pulls it downward.
And pushes the ball with his foot so it will return immediately, and if you wish you may reduce it in this way: bring a man taller than the patient and position him at the side and have him insert his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raise his armpit upward until the patient is suspended in the air.
Another assistant pulls the patient's hand down toward their belly, and if the patient is light in weight, something else should be attached to weigh them down, as the dislocation will be reduced immediately, and it can also be reduced in another way.
A long wooden post should be fixed in the ground with a rounded head like a mortar pestle, neither too thick nor too thin, then placed under the patient's armpit after putting soft rags on the post's head, with the patient standing along the length of the post.
Then their hand is pulled downward from the other side and their body is also pulled strongly from the other direction, so the joint quickly returns to its place. If reduction is difficult with all we mentioned, then use this treatment: take a wooden piece about two cubits long.
Its width should be about four fingers and its thickness about two fingers, with a rounded head to facilitate its entry into the depth of the armpit, as shown in this illustration:
Then tie soft rags on the rounded head so the wood doesn't harm the patient, then place it under the armpit and extend the entire hand and arm down along the wood, and tie the wood to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then place the arm across a ladder rung and pull the hand downward, leaving the rest of the body hanging from the other side, and the joint will reduce immediately. When reduction is complete by whatever means possible, a moderately-sized wool ball should be placed under the armpit.
Then apply the dressing prepared from mill dust with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder as it curves from above, then firmly bind the ball under the armpit and wrap the bandage over the dressing from above, and suspend the hand from their neck.
The patient should not move their hand for seven days, and their food should be reduced until the limb strengthens, as this speeds recovery. Then untie after seven or five days and exercise with movement - if it remains stable without loosening, it has healed.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture affecting it or another cause, then cauterization should be performed with three skewers as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. If you do all this and remove the bandage after seven days and the joint doesn't stay in place.
And you repeat the dressing and binding multiple times but it doesn't stay and falls and becomes lax and you cannot raise it upward, know that its nerve at the head of the shoulder has either torn, stretched, or become lax, and know then that the joint will never stay in place.
As for the dislocation toward the chest and breast and backward, its reduction is done by pushing and pulling with hands until it returns, and all binding and treatment is used until it heals. If after healing there is stiffness in the limb and slowness in movement.
The patient should use the bath many times until that stiffness softens and returns to its natural state.
Chapter Twenty-Seven
On the Treatment of Elbow Dislocation
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and likewise returns with difficulty, and it can dislocate in all directions, especially forward or backward, and its dislocation is not hidden from you as it is visible and palpable in whatever form it dislocates.
When you compare the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, this becomes clearly evident to you by the joint's concavity, and the patient cannot bend the arm or touch their shoulder with it. You should hurry to reduce the dislocation immediately before hot swelling occurs.
For if hot swelling occurs, reduction becomes difficult and sometimes might not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backward, as it is the worst of all types of dislocations, the most painful, and often death descends with it.
Its reduction, when possible to return, requires an assistant to pull their hand with both hands while the arm is extended, and the physician's hands above and below the elbow while pushing the joint with both thumbs or the base of their palm until it returns to its place.
If the dislocation is forward, it can be reduced by suddenly bending the arm until the base of its palm strikes the shoulder that faces it. If the dislocation does not respond to reduction, then employ very strong and forceful traction, such that two assistants pull the arm.
Two assistants should also hold the patient lest he move during the traction. Then rotate the arm in all directions after wrapping his hand with a folded cloth or wide bandage. When the physician manipulates the joint with his hands, he should anoint them with oil to help the joint slide back easily.
Then push the joint forcefully until it returns. After its return, it is necessary to apply a dressing that contains astringents and drying agents along with egg white, bind it firmly, suspend the arm from the patient's neck, and leave it for days.
Then unbind it. If the joint has stabilized in its place, remove the binding and leave it. If you see the joint has not fully strengthened, reapply the dressing and binding and leave it again for days until it strengthens, then unbind it. If stiffness occurs after the joint's return and unbinding,
And movement becomes difficult, then moistening treatments should be used in the bath, along with gentle massage and kneading until it softens, or place the fat tail of a fat ram on the joint, then bind it and leave it for a day and night, then remove it and have him enter the bath. When he sweats, rub the joint moderately.
Then reapply the fat tail a second and third time with bath entry until it softens. If you wish to apply fresh cow dung mixed with fat and bind it on, do this repeatedly as it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight
On the Treatment of Wrist Dislocation
The wrist frequently dislocates, and reducing its dislocation is easier than other joints, but it is necessary to hasten its reduction in the hour it dislocates, before the area swells or hot swelling occurs.
To reduce its dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board, have an assistant extend his hand, and the physician places his palm on the joint's prominence and pushes until it returns. However, it is necessary to observe if the dislocation has moved to the palm of the hand.
Then the patient should place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction. If the dislocation protrudes to the back of the hand, then the palm should be placed on the board so the physician's hand falls directly on the joint's prominence. If it returns immediately, fine; if not, bind it with a dressing that prevents swelling.
Leave it and do not attempt again as it cannot bear it, and you cannot reduce it after days have passed, though the joint will remain crooked without harming the patient unless the hand becomes flaccid and cannot grasp anything.
At that point, know that the nerve is severed or crushed, and there is no solution except cauterization, which may or may not help. When the wrist reduction is complete, apply the dressing we described, then bind it and leave it for five days.
Then unbind it and exercise the hand. If its movement becomes difficult and some stiffness occurs, soften it with heated water and rubbing repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine
On the Treatment of Finger Dislocations
Fingers can dislocate in any direction. When a finger dislocates toward the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns, then bind the fingertip and suspend it toward the direction it was dislocated to.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it stands straight that day. At night, bind it according to the same description. Continue to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night. Do this for days until it strengthens.
Do likewise if it dislocates toward the palm of the hand - bind it toward the same direction and treat it as you did the first time until it heals. Do the same whenever it dislocates in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
On treating dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs in one of the vertebrae of the back or neck, or when many vertebrae move from their positions, there is no treatment for it because death comes quickly to the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's stool comes out involuntarily without being able to control it, and often some of their limbs become paralyzed, either their legs, their arms, or one of them. But if a single vertebra moves from its position, it can often be restored.
Its displacement can be in four directions, and that which moves backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should observe - if the hump has occurred since childhood, there is no treatment for it and no cure whatsoever. As for that which occurred due to a fall or a blow or similar.
The ancients have mentioned various types of treatments with lengthy discourse, most of which is not useful, and I have summarized from that what little suffices instead of their many words, based on my experience with the matter and my explanation of it, and I have illustrated the instrument differently from what they showed and explained.
I say that the hump which occurs in the front of the chest has no solution and no cure, and likewise those to both sides also. Only that which occurs in the back specifically can be treated with what I am about to describe, which is that you lay the patient on his face.
On a level bench near a wall, and spread beneath him soft padding so his chest isn't hurt, then place an upright wooden post planted in a hole in the ground at the end of the bench near his head, and another post near his feet at the other end of the bench.
And an assistant holds the post, which should not be fixed in the hole, and another assistant holds the other one in the same way, then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft, secure bandage and extend the end of the bandage to the post at his head and tie it there.
Then secure with another bandage above his hips and above his knees and at his heel tendons, then gather all the bandages together and tie them to the other post which is at his feet, then each assistant pulls his post with the bandage in his direction, and the two posts do not move from their positions they are planted in.
Except that they are not fixed as we said, and the physician places his palms on the vertebra forcefully until it returns, or places a board on it then leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board about three cubits in length.
And dig in the wall which we said should be near the patient a place to insert the end of the board, then place the middle of the board on the hump and the physician places his feet on the other end and presses down firmly until he compresses the vertebra.
And it returns to its place. And if you wish to do it with the screw that is turned by hand, which is that you plant in the ground at the patient's head at the end of the bench two wooden posts one cubit in length, and at his feet at the end of the bench also two posts.
And let the distance between each post be a span, and make in each post a hole through which the screw runs, and secure all the posts firmly in the ground so they do not move at all, and insert a rounded stick which is the screw on which the bandage is wound through the holes of the two posts.
And in its end a hole in which is secured a stick a span in length with which to turn it, and in the other two posts likewise, then tie the bandage which you secured on the patient's chest to the screw at his head and the bandages which you secured on his legs to the screw at his feet.
Then an assistant stands at each screw turning it with his hand using the handle which turns the screw while the physician aligns the hump as we described before, and this is the illustration of the screw and the bench and the patient:
Then after the vertebra returns and the area becomes level, you should apply the drying dressing with egg white then the oakum then place above the dressing a splint of wood that is three fingers wide or so and its length should be enough to cover the area of the hump.
And on some of the healthy vertebrae, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses mild nourishment until healing. If some protrusion remains in the area after healing, medicines that relax and soften should be used along with the board we described for a long time.
A lead plate may be used for this, and a protrusion may appear in the last vertebrae of the back which might be thought to be a dislocation, but it is an additional protruding bone, so do not attempt this treatment for it as death may occur.
Chapter Thirty-One
On the Treatment of the Dislocated Hip
Know that the hip joint and shoulder joint only experience dislocation and do not experience what other joints experience in terms of slight displacement and concavity. The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
It dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward, most commonly inward and rarely forward or backward. The sign of its dislocation inward is that when you compare the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, it will be longer.
The knee will protrude more than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his leg at the groin, and the area near the groin will be notably swollen because the head of the femur has moved there.
The signs of outward dislocation are opposite to these symptoms, and the sign of forward dislocation is that the patient can extend their leg completely but cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if they attempt to walk, they cannot move forward.
Their urine is retained, their groin swells, and when walking, they step on the heel. The sign of backward dislocation is that they cannot extend or bend the knee before bending the groin, and their leg is also shorter than the other.
The groin is relaxed and the head of the femur is clearly visible at the flank area. As for reducing these types of dislocations, if the dislocation is old and chronic, and reduction hasn't been attempted and it remained in its condition, there is absolutely no treatment.
It should not be attempted. But if the dislocation is recent, regardless of which of the four types it is, proceed to twist the joint and extend it inward and outward and move it right and left, as it may
return without needing other treatment. If it doesn't return this way, you should prepare a strong, capable assistant to pull the leg from below either with his hands or with a bandage tied above the knee, and another assistant pulls from above.
By putting his hands under the armpits, then securing a soft bandage at the base of the thigh, and a third assistant holds the end of the bandage, pulling either from the front from the groin area toward the clavicle, or from behind toward the back.
They should all pull at once until the patient's body rises from the ground and remains suspended, as this type of traction is common to all four types. If the dislocation returns with what we mentioned, fine, otherwise each type needs its own specific treatment that I will mention.
As for the specific reduction when the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on their healthy side, then place a bandage at the base of the thigh between the head of the femur and the area below the groin.
Then pull the bandage upward from the groin area to the upper body toward the clavicle, then another strong, capable assistant should embrace the thick part of the affected thigh with his arms and pull strongly outward, and it will return to its place.
This type is easier than other types of treatment for reducing this limb. If it proves difficult and doesn't respond to insertion with this type of treatment at all, you should bind both legs of the patient with a strong, soft bandage at the ankles and knees.
And each one should be four fingers apart from their companion, and the afflicted leg should be extended more than the other by about two fingers, then the patient is suspended from the head from a wooden beam in the house, being about two forearms' length from the ground.
Then order a strong servant to embrace the head of the thigh, and another servant to hold onto the patient, and the other servant who is embracing the thigh pushes with force, for the joint will quickly return to its place. As for its specific reduction when the dislocation is outward:
The patient should lie on the bench as we described for the person with kyphosis, and the bandage should be tied specifically on his afflicted leg and on his chest, then two wooden pieces are placed - one at his feet and the other at his head - then an additional wooden piece is placed in the middle of the bench, very securely fastened.
It should be wrapped with moist rags so as not to harm the patient, to be between his thighs so he doesn't get pulled downward during the extension. Then each servant pulls to his side while the physician aligns the hip with his hands. If it responds by returning,
And if not, place the board on it and compress it as we mentioned for kyphosis exactly, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side. And if the dislocation is forward, the patient's leg should be extended at once while he is in this same position on the bench.
The physician places his right palm on the afflicted groin then presses it with the other hand, and with that makes the pressure extend downward toward the knee. And if the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be extended downward.
While elevated from the ground, rather he should be placed on something solid, as should also be done for one whose hip is dislocated outward, as we mentioned regarding his lying on the bench while on his face with the bandages tied.
As we said just now, and compression with the board should also be used on the location to which the joint has moved. When the reduction of the hip joint is completed properly - and the sign of its reduction will not be hidden from you - you should extend the patient's legs.
If you see them even and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned properly. At that point, join the thighs together and apply the dressing and bind with a turban so tightly that the hip cannot move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days, then undo the binding and dressing and compare the leg with the other. If you see them equal in length, know that the dislocation has set, so allow the patient to walk. And if you see any looseness in it,
Return and dress it and bind it as you bound it the first time and leave it again for three days, then undo it, and he should walk on it slowly for days until it strengthens well, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Two
On Treating Dislocation of the Knee
The knee dislocates in three ways: it dislocates inward, outward, and downward - meaning backward - and it never dislocates forward at all. The sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to join his leg to his thigh.
If it doesn't attach to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. And the reduction of all types of its dislocation is that you have the patient sit while extending his legs if he is able to do that, and have a servant sit behind him to hold his waist and bend him backward slightly, then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back to his face and put his leg between your legs, then grip his knee with your palms and interlock your fingers over his knee, then squeeze the sides of his knee strongly with your palms while another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg attaches to the thigh smoothly without force. Then dress it and attach the leg to the thigh, then bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release them, and he should only walk a little for days until it strengthens.
If its return proves difficult with what we described, then use strong extension with the bandages which I previously described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of ankle dislocation.
The ankle may become slightly displaced or completely dislocated, and its dislocation can be either inward or outward, and the sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen and protruding in the direction of the dislocation.
As for treating its displacement, its reduction is easy: it should be gently pulled by hand and adjusted until it returns, but as for treating it when it is completely dislocated, the patient should be seated and a strong assistant should hold him from behind at his waist.
Then you should hold his foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand from below his foot at the location of the Achilles tendon, then pull the foot toward you with your right hand then with your left hand twice, then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand toward the leg without force.
Do this twice as we described, then push the front of the foot toward the leg the third time while pulling the Achilles tendon, and if it returns after one or two times in this manner and you see the foot straight, fine, otherwise repeat the procedure for it will return.
If its return is prevented by what we described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and drive a peg firmly into the ground to fall between his thighs, having wrapped it with rags so as not to harm the patient, then an assistant should hold his thigh.
Then another assistant pulls the leg either with his hands or with a bandage tied to the neck of the leg, then each assistant pulls opposite to his companion's pull while the peg stands between the patient's thighs holding him so his body is not drawn downward during the pulling, then the physician adjusts the dislocation with his hands.
And another assistant holds the healthy leg downward for the dislocation will return quickly, and when the dislocation returns and you confirm its proper return, apply the dressing and lint and bind it with firm bandages and secure the foot with the bandage downward, and you should be cautious of the nerve that is above the ankle from behind.
Lest the bandage be too tight on it and harm it, then leave it for two or three days, and if the bandage loosens then tighten it, then release it on the third or fourth day, and the patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, you cannot be sure that the dislocation won't recur and deteriorate and not accept treatment afterward, and if hot swelling occurs, you should use for its relief what we previously described in other places regarding treatment and fomentation until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On treating toe dislocations.
You should adjust what has been dislocated with slight pulling without force, and this is not difficult but easy, and if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the top of the foot, the patient should place his foot on a level place on the ground.
Or on a board while standing as if walking, then you stand and place your foot on what protrudes from those joints then press it with your foot firmly until it returns and you see it has straightened and no protrusion appears in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that takes the whole foot having two ends, then bind it with a firm, secure binding for three days then unfasten it and protect it from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are assured against recurrence, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On types of dislocations that occur with wounds or with fractures or with both together.
When any of this occurs and you attempt to treat and set it, it often results in death.
Therefore, none should attempt to treat such cases except one who is skilled in the craft, of long experience, gentle, compassionate, patient, neither rash nor bold, and should use at the beginning only medications that calm hot swellings and entrust the patient.
To divine decree, except for those cases where you hope for safety from harm with mild illness and some hope appears to you in it, then attempt its reduction immediately at the start before hot swelling occurs.
If the limb returns as you intended, then use the regimen that calms hot swellings and treat the wound with what suits it from drying ointments, and if the dislocation is with fracture and splinters appear in the bone protruding, attempt to remove them.
And follow in that what we mentioned regarding simple diseases from what preceded in their places.
And do your utmost and keep yourself from entering the path of risk as per my previous advice to you, for that is more lasting for your status and safer for your reputation, God willing.
